Scope of Negation and Genuine Contradiction
Author explains scope conditions for contradictory opposition with modal and compound predicates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given pair of propositions with modal qualifiers or multiple predicate attributes are truly contradictory, based on the scope of the negation particle.

Core Idea
The negation particle must scope over the entire modal or compound predicate for genuine contradiction; otherwise both can be false.

In Islamic logic, a key skill is identifying whether two propositions genuinely contradict each other. This passage teaches you how to probe the scope of negation when the predicate includes modal qualifiers (like 'necessarily') or multiple attributes (like 'skilled, sincere'). Misidentifying scope leads to false contradictions, a common error in argumentation. We will unpack the author's conditions step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two propositions in the pair
Look for an affirmative and a negative proposition that are being compared. Note the exact wording, especially where the negation particle (laysa, ma, etc.) is placed.
2
Locate the modal qualifier (if any)
If a modal expression like 'bi-al-darura' (necessarily) appears, check whether the negation comes before or after it. If negation is after the mode, the pair can both be false. True contradiction requires negation before the modal phrase.
Example: 'Zayd is necessarily a doctor' vs 'Zayd is necessarily not a doctor' can both be false because the mode might be wrong; the underlying relation could be possibility.
3
Examine compound predicates (multiple attributes)
If the predicate has multiple attributes (e.g., 'skilled, sincere'), see which part the negation applies to. If negation only applies to some attributes, the pair can both be false. True contradiction requires negation to apply to the whole compound predicate.
Example: 'Zayd is a skilled sincere doctor' vs 'Zayd is a doctor not skilled sincere' – both can be false if 'doctor' does not apply to Zayd at all.
4
Check for quantifier differences
If the propositions are quantified, universal opposites (e.g., 'Every animal is human' and 'No animal is human') can both be false when the predicate applies to some but not all. Particular subcontraries (e.g., 'Some animal is human' and 'Some animal is not human') can both be true. Only when one is universal and the other particular do we have genuine contradictory opposition where one is true and the other false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

والأول كما في قولنا في الموجبة زيد) هو بالضرورة طبيب وفي السالبة (زيد بالضرورة ليس طبيباً بتأخير حرف السلب عن الجهة وتقديمه على المحمول، فإنهما لا يتكاذبان بل جاز اجتماعهما في الكذب؛ لجواز أن تكون بالضرورة طبيباً)، والثاني فكما في قولنا في الموجبة زيد طبيب ماهر ناصح وفي السالبة (زيد طبيب ليس بماهر ناصح) أو (زيد طبيـب مـاهـر لـيس بناصح فإنه لا تكاذب بينهما؛ لجواز أن يكون الكذب فيهما إنما هو لنسبة الطبيب إليه، والطبيب منسوب إليه فيهما ، بل إنما يتكاذبان أن لو كان حرف السلب داخلاً على الجميع كما في قولنا : زيد) ليس بطبيب ماهر (ناصح وإن لم يتعين أن ما رفعه السلب هل هو كل ذلك الموجبة أو بعضه؟ من أنه ليس بماهر أو ليس بناصح أو اثنين منها، بل يجعل المحمول بصفاته شيئاً واحداً، ثم يقال إن هذا المحمول من هذا المحمول ليس للموضوع، وهذا كله مما لا يختلف فيه شيء من القضايا الشخصية والمهملة والمحصورة ، بل إن كانت محصورة زيد على ما أشرنا إليه الاختلاف في الكم وإلا فالكليتان المتضادتان قد تجتمعان في الكذب عند كون المحمول صادقاً في الإيجاب على بعض الموضوع، وكاذباً على كله كما في قولنا: كل حيوان إنسان (لا) واحد من الحيوان إنسان والجزئيتان الداخلتان تحت المتضادتين قد تجتمعان في الصدق لما أشرنا إليه من المعنى في الكليتين كما إذا قلنا (بعض الحيوان إنسان (بعض الحيوان ليس إنساناً فإذن مستيقن كان البعض الآخر كاذباً أو صادقاً، إذ ليس من ضرورة كذب الكل كذب البعض، وليس من ضرورة كذب البعض كذب الكل، ومن ضرورة الكذب في البعض عند كون الكلي موجباً صدق السلب عن البعض لا عن الكل، ومن الكذب في البعض عند كون الكلي سالباً صدق الإيجاب في البعض لا في الكل، هذا إن كانت القضية خبرية فيلزم من كذبها إن كانت موجبة صدق السلب الكلي، وإلا ما كان البعض الموجب
As for what relates to the negation particle, it is that a mode is added to the predicate itself, and the negation particle does not enter upon it, but is mentioned after it and before the predicate; or that attributes are added to it, and the negation particle only enters upon them or some of them. The first is like our saying in the affirmative: 'Zayd is necessarily a doctor', and in the negative: 'Zayd is necessarily not a doctor', by placing the negation particle after the mode and before the predicate. Then they are not contradictory; rather it is possible for both to be false together, because the relation of doctor to Zayd could be by possibility, not necessity, while the mode in both is stated as necessity. They are only contradictory if the negation particle enters upon the mode and the predicate, as in our saying: 'Zayd is not necessarily a doctor'. The second is like our saying in the affirmative: 'Zayd is a skilled, sincere doctor', and in the negative: 'Zayd is a doctor not skilled, sincere' or 'Zayd is a skilled doctor not sincere'. Then there is no contradiction between them, because it is possible that the falsehood in both is due to the attribution of doctor to him, and the doctor is attributed to him in both. Rather, they are only contradictory if the negation particle enters upon the whole, as in our saying: 'Zayd is not a skilled sincere doctor', even if it is not specified what the negation removes—whether all of that affirmative or some of it—whether he is not skilled or not sincere or both. Rather, the predicate with its attributes is made one thing, then it is said: this predicate is not for the subject. And all of this does not differ in any of the particular, indefinite, or quantified propositions. Rather, if it is quantified, there is added the difference in quantity as we indicated. Otherwise, the two universal opposites may both be false together when the predicate is true in the affirmative for some of the subject and false for all of it, as in our saying: 'Every animal is a human' and 'No animal is a human'. The two particulars under the opposites may both be true together, due to the meaning we indicated for the universals, as when we say: 'Some animal is a human' and 'Some animal is not a human'. So their division of truth and falsehood only occurs if they differ in quantity such that one is universal and the other particular. For when the universal is false, whether affirmative or negative, its falsehood may be due to the falsehood of every single one of its individuals, or due to the falsehood of some. So falsehood is certain whether the other part is false or true, since it is not necessary that if the whole is false the part is false, nor that if the part is false the whole is false. And it is necessary from falsehood in the part, when the universal is affirmative, the truth of the negative about the part not about the whole; and from falsehood in the part, when the universal is negative, the truth of the affirmative in the part not in the whole. This is if the proposition is declarative. Then from its falsehood, if it is affirmative, the truth of the universal negative follows; otherwise, the part that is affirmative…
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage explains how the placement of the negation particle affects whether a contradictory pair is genuine. The author distinguishes two subcases: when a modal expression (e.g., 'necessarily') is added to the predicate, and when attributes are added. In the first subcase, if the negation particle comes after the mode (e.g., 'Zayd is necessarily not a doctor'), the pair can both be false because the mode might be incorrectly assigned while the underlying relation is possible. True contradiction requires the negation to enter on the mode and predicate together (e.g., 'Zayd is not necessarily a doctor'). In the second subcase, if the predicate has multiple attributes and the negation affects only some (e.g., 'Zayd is a doctor not skilled' vs. 'Zayd is a skilled doctor'), they can both be false because the affirmative may be false due to the predicate not applying at all, while the negative is false by denying a part that is true. Only when negation covers the entire predicate with attributes do we have a true contradiction. The passage then shifts to quantified propositions, explaining that universal opposites (e.g., 'Every animal is human' and 'No animal is human') can both be false when the predicate applies to some but not all. Similarly, particular opposites (e.g., 'Some animal is human' and 'Some animal is not human') can both be true. The general rule is that contradictory opposition occurs only when the propositions differ in quantity (one universal, one particular) or when the negation is properly scoped. The author ends by stating that if a declarative proposition is false, then its contradictory is true, but this is nuanced for the parts.

Key Terms
جهة
الكليتان المتضادتان
الجزئيتان الداخلتان تحت المتضادتين

Checkpoint
Consider this pair: Affirmative: 'Zayd is a skilled sincere doctor.' Negative: 'Zayd is a doctor not skilled sincere.' Why can both be false simultaneously?
A) Because the negation applies to the whole predicate, not part of it.
B) Because 'doctor' may be falsely attributed to Zayd in both, while the attributes differ.
C) Because the mode is different in each.
D) Because the quantity is different.

Key Takeaway
True contradiction requires that the negation particle scope over the entire modal qualifier or compound predicate, and for quantified propositions, one must be universal and the other particular.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the pair: 'Zayd is necessarily just' and 'Zayd is necessarily not just'. Determine if they are truly contradictory according to the passage. Explain why or why not.
?
Construct a pair of compound predicate propositions (affirmative and negative) such that they ARE truly contradictory, following the author's condition that negation scopes over the entire compound predicate. Use an Islamic example (e.g., 'The caliph is a just, wise leader' and something negative).

Contradictories of Permanent and Absolute Propositions
Analysis of contradiction between permanent and absolute propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the true contradictory of a given permanent or absolute categorical proposition and determine whether two such propositions can be both false or both true.

Core Idea
Permanent and absolute propositions are not simply contradictories; their logical relationship depends on temporal conditions.

In this passage, the author unpacks the complex relationship between permanent (dā’im) and general absolute (muṭlaq ‘āmm) propositions. He shows that the possibility of mutual falsity (takādhub) or mutual truth between them hinges on whether the absolute proposition includes necessary or merely possible existence. You will learn how to construct the correct contradictory for each type of proposition and why the 'trick' of adding a temporal condition often fails.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish permanent vs. absolute propositions
Permanent (dā'im) propositions hold for all times, either necessarily or accidentally. General absolute (muṭlaq 'āmm) propositions hold at least once without temporal condition. For example, 'Zayd is white' (absolute) vs. 'Zayd is permanently white' (permanent).
2
Understand the condition for mutual falsity (takādhub)
The author argues that mutual falsity between a permanent and a general absolute occurs only when what is included under the absolute is necessary existence (lā budda min wujūdihi), not merely possible existence. If the absolute includes possibility, the permanent may be false while the absolute is true.
This condition prevents automatic subcontrariety.
3
Apply the rule for negative absolute propositions
For a negative absolute like 'absolutely, Zayd is not a writer', its true contradictory is a permanent affirmative: 'Zayd is always a writer'. This is because the contradictory must differ in truth value in all cases.
This follows from the definition of true contradictory (naqīd ḥaqīqī).
4
Analyze the trick of adding a temporal condition
The 'best of the later scholars' suggests making the proposition more specific by adding a condition like 'as long as it is B' to generate a contradictory of the same type. However, the author shows this can fail: both can be false if the predicate holds only sometimes, or both true if the condition is absent.
Example: 'Every pleuritic coughs as long as he is pleuritic' and 'Some pleuritic does not cough as long as he is pleuritic' can both be false if some cough only some of the time.
5
Find the correct contradictory
The author provides a working solution: for 'every B is C as long as it is B', the true contradictory is 'some B as long as it is B is not C with general absolute'. This differs in type (universal vs. particular) but shares the genus of absolute proposition, ensuring proper contradiction.
This resolves the earlier scholar's statement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أبيض) عندما إذا كان دائم السواد زيد بالضرورة ليس بأبيض) والدائم الاتفاقي وإن كان ممكناً والممكن أخص من المطلق العام، ويلزم من كذب الأعمّ كذب الأخص فإنما يمتنع التكاذب بين الدائم والمطلق العام أن لو كان مطلق الإمكان داخلاً تحت المطلق العام إذ يلزم من كذب مطلق الإمكان كذب الدائم الممكن وليس كذلك، بل الداخل تحت المطلق من الممكن ليس إلا ما لا بد من وجوده لا ما من وجوده بد، وإذ ذاك فالتكاذب واقع لا محالة، هذا إن كانت موجبة، وإن كانت سالبة كقولنا : (بالإطلاق زيد ليس بكاتب فنقيضها الحقيقي (زيد ليس بالإطلاق ليس بكاتب بل دائماً زيد كاتب، وإن كانت المطلقة محصورة فإما كليّة أو جزئية، فإن كانت كلية فنقيضها الحقيقي ما عرف ولازمه دائماً ليس كل "ب" "ج") إن كانت القضية بالإطلاق ليس كل "ب" "ج"). والمهملات حكمها حكم الجزئيات، قال أفضل المتأخرين فإن أردنا أن نجد للمطلقة نقيضاً من جنسها كانت الحيلة فيه أن نجعل القضية أخص مما يوجبه نفس الإيجاب أو السلب المطلقان، وذلك بأن يكون الحكم بالمحمول على الموضوع ما دام متصفاً بالشرط المأخوذ مع الموضوع، وذلك إما في الكلي الموجب بأن نقول (كل "ب" "ج" ما دام "ب" وعلى هذا النحو في باقي القضايا المحصورة. وفيه نظر: فإن النقيض إن كان عرفياً من نوع القضية المذكورة لم يتكاذبا؛ لجواز اجتماعهما في الكذب إذا كان المحمول ثابتاً للموضوع في بعض أوقات كونه متصفاً بما وصف به لا دائماً كقولنا (كل مجنوب يسعل ما دام مجنوباً) (بعض المجنوب لا يسعل ما دام مجنوباً) وإن كان مطلقاً عاماً فلا تكاذب أيضاً؛ لجواز اجتماعهما في الصدق بأن يكون الإيجاب صادقاً حالة دوام الصفة، والسلب صادقاً عند عدمها، وإنما يقع التكاذب عند كون النقيض مطلقاً عاماً شرط بقاء موضوعه على الوجه العرفي، وذلك بأن نقول نقيض كل "ب" "ج" ما دام بعض "ب" ما دام "ب" ليس "ج" بالإطلاق العام وهما وإن اختلفا نوعاً فمتحدان جنساً لصدق المطلقة العامة عليهما، ويجب أن يحمل عليه قول أفضل المتأخرين.
(White) when if Zayd is permanently black, then necessarily Zayd is not white. And the accidental permanent, although it is possible, and the possible is more specific than the general absolute, and from the falsity of the more general follows the falsity of the more specific, so the impossibility of mutual falsity between the permanent and the general absolute would only be if absolute possibility is included under the general absolute, for then from the falsity of absolute possibility would follow the falsity of the possible permanent, but it is not so. Rather, what is included under the absolute from the possible is only that whose existence is necessary, not that from which existence is free. And if that is the case, then mutual falsity occurs inevitably. This is if it is affirmative. And if it is negative, like our saying: 'absolutely, Zayd is not a writer', then its true contradictory is 'Zayd is not absolutely not a writer, but rather Zayd is always a writer'. And if the absolute proposition is quantified, then either universal or particular. If it is universal, its true contradictory is what is known, and its necessary consequence is always 'not every B is C' if the proposition is absolutely 'not every B is C'. And unquantified propositions have the same rule as particular ones. The best of the later scholars said: if we want to find for the absolute proposition a contradictory of its own kind, the trick is to make the proposition more specific than what the absolute affirmation or negation itself requires, namely by judging the predicate of the subject as long as it is described by the condition taken with the subject. This is either in the universal affirmative by saying 'every B is C as long as it is B', and in this manner in the rest of the quantified propositions. But there is a consideration: for if the contradictory is conventional of the same type as the mentioned proposition, they are not mutually false; because it is possible for them to both be false if the predicate is true of the subject at some times of its being described as such but not always, like our saying: 'every pleuritic coughs as long as he is pleuritic' (and) 'some pleuritics do not cough as long as he is pleuritic'. And if it is general absolute, then also no mutual falsity; because it is possible for them to both be true, by the affirmation being true at the time of the permanence of the attribute and the negation being true at its absence. Mutual falsity only occurs when the contradictory is a general absolute conditioned on the continued existence of its subject in the conventional manner, such that we say the contradictory of 'every B is C as long as it is B' is 'some B as long as it is B is not C with general absolute'. And although they differ in type, they are united in genus because the general absolute proposition applies to both. And upon this should be interpreted the statement of the best of the later scholars.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage analyzes the logical relationship between permanent (dā’im) propositions and general absolute (muṭlaq ‘āmm) propositions, specifically the conditions under which they can be true contraries or subcontraries. The author begins by noting that an accidental permanent proposition is possible, and the possible is more specific than the general absolute. From the falsity of the more general, the falsity of the more specific follows. The reason mutual falsity (takādhub) between the permanent and the general absolute is not always possible is that absolute possibility (muṭlaq al-imkān) is not entirely included under the general absolute; only that which must exist (lā budda min wujūdihi) is included, not that which may or may not exist. When this condition is met, mutual falsity does occur. The author then distinguishes affirmative and negative cases. For negative absolute propositions, the true contradictory is a permanent affirmative. For quantified absolute propositions, the contradictory for a universal is 'not every B is C'. Unquantified propositions (muhmalaat) follow the rule of particulars. The author then cites the 'best of the later scholars' (afḍal al-muta’akhkhirīn) who suggests a trick: to make the proposition more specific by adding a condition that the predicate holds as long as the subject is described by a certain attribute (e.g., 'every B is C as long as it is B'). This yields a contradictory of the same type. However, the author raises a criticism: if the contradictory is conventional (‘urfī) and of the same type, they can both be false (e.g., 'every pleuritic coughs as long as he is pleuritic' and 'some pleuritics do not cough as long as he is pleuritic' can both be false if the predicate is true only sometimes). If the contradictory is general absolute, they can both be true. True mutual falsity occurs only when the contradictory is a general absolute conditioned on the continued existence of the subject in the conventional manner—then the propositions are truly contradictory in the sense that they cannot both be true or both false. The example given: the contradictory of 'every B is C as long as it is B' is 'some B as long as it is B is not C with general absolute'. These differ in type (universal vs. particular) but share the genus of absolute proposition. This resolves the earlier scholar's statement.

Key Takeaway
The true contradictory of a general absolute proposition is a permanent proposition, but the conditions for mutual falsity depend on whether the absolute includes necessary or merely possible existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Contradiction of Special Absolute Propositions
Author explains the true contradictory for special absolute propositions, both singular and quantified.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to find the true contradictory of any special absolute proposition (singular, universal, or particular) and identify its permanent consequent.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a special absolute proposition always implies a permanent proposition.

Special absolute propositions (bil-wujūd) carry a temporal nuance: they assert something holds at some time, not always. When we contradict them, we don't simply negate – we uncover a permanent truth. This lesson teaches you to derive that permanent consequence for singular, universal, and particular cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type and quantity
Determine if the proposition is singular (makhṣūṣa) or quantified (universal or particular). This determines how the contradictory is formed and the nature of the consequent.
Singular: e.g., 'Zayd is existentially a writer'. Universal: 'existentially every B is C'. Particular: 'existentially some B is C'.
2
Formulate the true contradictory
For singular affirmative, the contradictory is a negative with double negation: 'Zayd is not existentially not a writer'. For universal affirmative, it is 'not existentially every B is C'. For particular affirmative, it is 'not existentially some B is C'.
Negate the whole proposition, not just the modality.
3
Analyze the meaning of falsity
Since 'bil-wujūd' means 'at some time, not always', falsity can arise from either the temporal part ('at some time') or the 'not always' part. This leads to a permanent truth.
The falsity of the special absolute does not simply yield its opposite; it forces a permanent claim.
4
Derive the necessary consequent
For singular, the consequent is a permanent singular (always Zayd is writer or always Zayd is not writer). For universal, a permanent particular (always some B is C or always some B is not C). For particular, a permanent universal (always every B is C or always no B is C), but distributive, not collective.
The consequent is always a permanent (dā’im) proposition.
5
Recognize that permanence suffices
The consequent may be necessary or accidental, but it is always permanent. Every necessary is permanent, but not every permanent is necessary. Permanence is the key logical consequence.
Do not require necessity; permanence is sufficient for logical purposes.

وأما إن كان الإطلاق بالمعنى الخاصي فإما أن تكون القضية أيضاً مخصوصة أو محصورة فإن كانت مخصوصة فنقيضها الحقيقي لموجبتها وهي زيد بالوجود كاتب) (زيد ليس بالوجود ليس بكاتب ولازمه في كلا الحالين إما دائماً زيد كاتب، وإما دائماً زيد ليس كاتباً، لأنا إذا قلنا بالوجود زيد كاتب كان معناه وقت ما لا دائماً، وكذا إذا قلنا بالوجود ليس بكاتب أي في وقت ما لا دائماً، فكذبهما إما لقولنا في وقت فيصدق الإيجاب الدائم إن كانت سالبة، أو السلب الدائم إن كانت موجبة، وإما لقولنا لا دائماً فيصدق الإيجاب الدائم إن كانت موجبة أو السلب الدائم إن كانت سالبة. والدوام في المخصوصات، وإن اتفق إن كان ضرورياً، فقد يكون اتفاقياً، وقد بان أن كل ضروري دائم، وليس كل دائم ضرورياً، فكان الدائم هو الصادق دون الضروري، وإن كانت محصورة فإما كليّة أو جزئية، فإن كانت كلية فنقيضها الحقيقي لموجبتها، وهو (بالوجود كل "ب" "ج") (ليس بالوجود كل "ب" "ج") ولازمه في كلا الحالتين أيضاً إما (دائماً بعض "ب" "ج") وإما (دائماً ليس بعض "ب" "ج") إذ من ضرورة الكذب في البعض الكذب في الكل كما عرف فالبعض متيقن، ولما كان الكذب في البعض متيقناً فإما أن يكون الكذب لقولنا: (في وقت) أو لقولنا: (لا) دائماً) ويلزم من ذلك أن يكون الصادق إما دوام الإيجاب في البعض أو دوام السلب في البعض كما سبق، وقد بان أن الدوام في البعض قد يكون ضرورياً، وقد يكون اتفاقياً، فكان الدوام أيضاً متيقناً وكافياً عن الضرورة. وإن كانت جزئية فنقيضها الحقيقي لموجبتها وهي (بالوجود بعض "ب" "ج") (ليس بالوجود بعض "ب" "ج") ولسالبتها، وهي (بالوجود ليس بعض "ب" "ج") (ليس بالوجود ليس بعض "ب" "ج") ولازمه في كلا الحالتين أيضاً دائماً إما (كل "ب" "ج") وإما (دائماً لا شيء من "ب" "ج") أي كل واحد واحد لا الكل جملة، إذ هو قد يكذب مع فرض كذب النقيض كما في قولنا : بالوجود بعض العدد زوج) فإنه يكذب لكونه ضرورياً، ومع كذبه يكذب كل عدد زوج) و(كل عدد ليس بزوج) بل اللازم دائماً (كل) (عدد) أي كل واحد واحد لا الكل جملة إما زوج وإما فرد؛
And if the absoluteness is according to the special meaning, then the proposition is either singular or quantified. If it is singular, its true contradictory for its affirmative 'Zayd is existentially a writer' is 'Zayd is not existentially not a writer' and its necessary consequent in both cases is either 'always Zayd is a writer' or 'always Zayd is not a writer'. Because if we say 'existentially Zayd is a writer', its meaning is 'at some time, not always', and likewise if we say 'existentially he is not a writer', i.e., at some time, not always. So their falsity is either due to our saying 'at some time' – then the permanent affirmative is true if it was negative, or the permanent negative if it was affirmative – or due to our saying 'not always' – then the permanent affirmative is true if it was affirmative, or the permanent negative if it was negative. And the permanence in singulars, although if it happens it may be necessary, it may also be accidental, and it has been shown that every necessary is permanent, but not every permanent is necessary. So the permanent is the true one, not the necessary. And if it is quantified, then either universal or particular. If it is universal, its true contradictory for its affirmative, which is 'existentially every B is C' is 'not existentially every B is C', and its necessary consequent in both cases is either 'always some B is C' or 'always some B is not C'. For from the necessity of falsity in some follows falsity in all, as is known, so the particular is certain. And since the falsity in the particular is certain, then either the falsity is due to our saying 'at some time' or due to our saying 'not always'. From that follows that the true is either permanent affirmation in the particular or permanent negation in the particular, as before. And it has been shown that the permanence in the particular may be necessary or accidental, so the permanence is also certain and suffices instead of necessity. And if it is particular, its true contradictory for its affirmative, which is 'existentially some B is C', is 'not existentially some B is C', and for its negative, which is 'existentially not some B is C', is 'not existentially not some B is C', and its necessary consequent in both cases is always either 'every B is C' or 'always none of B is C', i.e., each single one, not the whole collectively. For it may be false while assuming the falsity of the contradictory, as in our saying: 'existentially some number is even', for it is false because it is necessary, and with its falsity, 'every number is even' and 'every number is not even' are both false. Rather, the necessary consequent is always 'every number' – i.e., each single one – either even or odd.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage examines the 'special absolute' (al-muṭlaq bi al-ma‘nā al-khāṣṣī) proposition, which is defined by existential modality (bil-wujūd) meaning 'at some time, not always'. The author explains how to find its true contradictory and the necessary consequences that follow from the contradictory. The special absolute proposition is either singular (makhṣūṣa) or quantified (maḥṣūra). For singular propositions, the contradictory of an affirmative 'Zayd is existentially a writer' is 'Zayd is not existentially not a writer'. Both cases yield a permanent (dā’im) proposition as a necessary consequent: either 'always Zayd is a writer' or 'always Zayd is not a writer'. The reasoning is that the meaning of 'existentially' involves a temporal part ('at some time') and a negation of permanence ('not always'). Falsity of the special absolute can arise from either the temporal part or the 'not always' part, leading to a permanent truth. Importantly, the passage clarifies that this permanence may be necessary (ḍarūrī) or accidental (ittifāqī), but since every necessary is permanent (but not vice versa), it is the permanence that matters, not modality. For quantified propositions, the author handles two cases: universal and particular. For a universal special absolute affirmative 'existentially every B is C', its contradictory is 'not existentially every B is C'. Its necessary consequent is either 'always some B is C' or 'always some B is not C'. The reasoning: if the universal is false, at least one particular is false; since the falsity of a particular is certain, that falsity must be due either to a temporal condition or to a 'not always' condition, leading to a permanent truth in the particular. Again, permanence suffices, whether necessary or accidental. For a particular special absolute, whether affirmative or negative, the contradictory yields a universal permanent proposition: either 'always every B is C' or 'always no B is C'. The author clarifies that this universal is not a collective universal (kull jumla) but a distributive one (kull wāḥid wāḥid). An example from arithmetic illustrates: 'existentially some number is even' is false because it is necessary (some numbers are necessarily even), and when it is false, both 'every number is even' and 'every number is not even' are false. The necessary consequent is that every number (individually) is either even or odd – a permanent disjunction. In summary, the passage establishes that the contradictory of a special absolute proposition always implies a permanent proposition, which is certain enough for logical purposes.

Key Takeaway
The contradictory of any special absolute proposition always yields a permanent proposition, which is the key logical consequence for immediate inference.

Temporal Absolute Propositions and Their Contradictories
Author defines temporal absolute propositions and derives their contradictories and logical consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the real contradictory and the necessary consequent of any quantified temporal absolute proposition (universal or particular, affirmative or negative).

Core Idea
The contradictory of a temporal universal affirmative is a particular negative, not a universal negative.

Temporal absolute propositions assert a predicate for a subject at a specific time, without considering necessity or possibility, and without specifying whether the predicate holds for the entire time or part of it. Understanding how to find their contradictories is essential for constructing valid logical arguments in Islamic sciences, especially in debates about temporality and change.

Guided Path
1
Classify the proposition
First determine if the temporal proposition is specific (makhsusa) or quantified (mahsuma). If quantified, is it universal or particular? Then check the quality (affirmative or negative). This step follows the author's classification.
The passage details the branches: universal/p particular, each affirmative/negative.
2
Find the real contradictory
For a universal affirmative temporal like 'every B is J temporally', the real contradictory is 'not every B is J temporally', which is the negation of the whole proposition, not a universal negative. This maintains the same time qualification.
The contradictory must be opposite in quantity and quality while preserving the temporal condition.
3
Derive the necessary consequent (lāzim)
From the contradictory 'not every B is J temporally', the necessary consequent is that 'some B is not J in that supposed time'. This is because 'not every' implies existence of a counterexample within the time. Similarly for particular affirmatives.
The author also notes that if the contradictory is a particular negative, its contradictory (the original's consequent) is a universal affirmative, following the pattern of general absolute propositions.
4
Apply the same method to other cases
The passage leaves the reader to work out the particular negative and specific propositions. Follow the same classification and contradiction rules to find the contradictory and consequent for those cases.
The author says 'and it is upon you to consider the rest' encouraging independent practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان الإطلاق بالمعنى الوقتي فإما أن تكون الوقتية مخصوصة أو محصورة، فإن كانت محصورة فإما كليّة وإما جزئية، فإن كانت كليّة فإما موجبة وإما سالبة، فإن كانت كليّة موجبة كقولنا بالإطلاق الوقتي كل "ب" "ج" فنقيضها الحقيقي ليس بالإطلاق الوقتي كل "ب" "ج"، وأما لازم نقيضها فيجب أن يعلم أن القضية الوقتية هي ما كان المحمول فيها في نسبته إلى موضوعه في الوقت المفروض كحاله في نسبته إليه من غير اشتراط وقت وذلك من جهة عدم الالتفات في نسبته إلى الضرورة والإمكان أو أنه في جميع ذلك الوقت أو في بعضه، وعند ذلك فنقول اللازم بعض ما هو "ب" ليس هو "ج" في ذلك الوقت المفروض إما أنه جزئي سالب فالضرورة أن نقيضه كلي موجب لما عرف وإما أنه في جميع الوقت المفروض ليقع التكاذب على ما عرف في المطلقة العامة، وعلى هذا لا يخفى أمر السالبة الكلية، وإن كانت جزئية فإما موجبة وإما سالبة، فإن كانت موجبة كقولنا : بعض "ب" "ج" بالإطلاق الوقتي فنقيضها الحقيقي ليس بعض "ب" "ج" بالإطلاق الوقتي ولازمه كل "ب" "ج" في جميع الوقت المفروض، وتحققه على ما عرف وعليك باعتبار السالبة الجزئية وما بقي من المخصوصة بنفسك.
And as for if the absolute is in the temporal sense, then either the temporal is specific or quantified. If it is quantified, then either universal or particular. If it is universal, then either affirmative or negative. If it is universal affirmative, like our saying 'temporally absolutely every B is J', then its real contradictory is 'temporally absolutely not every B is J'. And as for the necessary consequent of its contradictory, it must be known that the temporal proposition is that whose predicate in its relation to its subject in the supposed time is like its state in relation to it without stipulating time, and that is due to not paying attention in its relation to necessity and possibility, or that it is in all that time or in some of it. And at that we say: the consequent is 'some B is not J in that supposed time'. Either it is a particular negative, so necessarily its contradictory is a universal affirmative as was known, or it is in all the supposed time so that contradiction occurs as was known in the general absolute. And on this, the case of the universal negative is not hidden. And if it is particular, then either affirmative or negative. If it is affirmative, like our saying 'some B is J with temporal absolute', then its real contradictory is 'not some B is J with temporal absolute', and its consequent is 'every B is J in all the supposed time', and its verification is as was known. And it is upon you to consider the particular negative and what remains of the specific propositions by yourself.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage defines temporal absolute propositions (al-mutlaq al-waqti) and explains how to find their real contradictories and necessary consequents. The author first classifies temporal propositions into specific (makhsusa) and quantified (mahsum). For quantified, they divide into universal and particular, then affirmative and negative. For the universal affirmative 'temporally every B is J', the real contradictory is 'not every B is J temporally', but the necessary consequent of that contradictory is a particular negative 'some B is not J in that supposed time'. This is because the contradictory must be in the same time condition. The author warns that the temporal proposition does not consider necessity/possibility or whether the predicate holds for the whole time or part—it just states the relation in the supposed time. For the particular affirmative, the contradictory is 'not some B is J', and the consequent is 'every B is J in all the supposed time'. The methodology mirrors that of the general absolute (mutlaq 'amma). The passage then leaves the analogous cases of the particular negative and specific propositions to the reader.

Modal Propositions and Their Real Contradictories
Explaining modal proposition contradictories and consequents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the real contradictory of any modal proposition and derive its necessary consequent using general possibility.

Core Idea
The real contradictory of a modal proposition negates the mode, not just the predicate.

Modal propositions add necessity or possibility to the subject-predicate relation. In classical logic, their contradictory is not a simple predicate negation but a negation of the mode itself. This passage from al-Daqā'iq al-Muḥkamah teaches the precise method: for any modal proposition, the real contradictory is formed by prefixing 'not necessarily' (or 'not possibly'), and from that contradictory we can deduce a necessary consequent using general possibility. Let's walk through the examples for universal and particular propositions.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the Modal Type
Identify whether the proposition is of necessity (ḍarūra) or possibility (imkān). The passage focuses on absolute necessity propositions, which are either universal, particular, or specific.
2
Form the Real Contradictory
For a necessity proposition, prefix 'not necessarily' (ليس بالضرورة) before the whole quantified statement. For example, the contradictory of 'necessarily every B is J' is 'not necessarily every B is J'. This preserves the mode and negates the necessity.
3
Derive the Necessary Consequent
The consequent of the contradictory is always a particular negative (for universal affirmative) or particular affirmative (for universal negative) with the mode 'general possibility (al-imkān al-‘āmm)'. For 'not necessarily every B is J', the consequent is 'by general possibility, not every B is J' (i.e., some B is not J by general possibility).
General possibility includes both necessary and possible negations.
4
Apply the Same Pattern to Other Quantifiers
For universal negative 'necessarily no B is J', the contradictory's consequent is 'by general possibility, some B is J'. For particular affirmative 'necessarily some B is J', the consequent is 'it is possible that no B is J in the general sense' (i.e., not all B are J by general possibility). Extend to particular negative and specific (المخصوصات) similarly.
5
Generalize the Rule
For any modal proposition, the real contradictory is formed by negating the mode ('not necessarily' for necessity, 'not possibly' for possibility), and its necessary consequent uses general possibility with the opposite quantity and quality according to the square of opposition.
This method works for both quantified and singular propositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كانت موجهة فالموجهة إما جهة الضرورة أو الإمكان، فإن كانت جهة الضرورة فإما أن تكون مطلقة أي غير مشروطة أو مشروطة، فإن كانت مطلقة فالقضية إما كليّة أو جزئية أو مخصوصة، فإن كانت كليّة فإما موجبة أو سالبة، فإن كانت موجبة كقولنا: بالضرورة كل "ب" "ج"، فنقيضها الحقيقي هو رفع محمولها عن موضوعها من جهة ما هو موجه من غير التفات إلى أنه هل الصادق رفع النسبة الإيجابية مع الجهة أو الجهة فقط، إذ من ضرورة الكذب في إثبات المحمول للموضوع بجهة صدق سلبه من جهة ما أثبت وهو أن يقال ليس بالضرورة كل "ب" "ج"، وعلى هذا النحو يكون النقيض الحقيقي لكل قضية موجهة كانت القضية محصورة أو شخصية موجبة أو سالبة والجهة ضرورية أو ممكنة بكل اعتبار، ولازم ليس بالضرورة كل "ب" "ج" بالإمكان العام ليس بعض "ب" "ج"، أما أنه جزئي سالب فلضرورة أنه نقيض الكلي الموجبة كما عرف، وأما أنه بالإمكان العام فلجواز أن يكون السلب في البعض بالضرورة أو بالإمكان العام لهما إنما هو الممكن العام، وعلى هذا إن كانت كلية سالبة كقولنا: بالضرورة لا شيء من "ب" "ج" فلازم نقيضها بالإمكان العام بعض "ب" "ج" لما حققناه. وإن كانت جزئية فإما موجبة وإما سالبة، فإن كانت موجبة كقولنا بالضرورة بعض "ب" "ج"، فنقيضها الحقيقي ليس بالضرورة بعض "ب" "ج" ولازمه ممكن أن لا يكون شيء من "ب" "ج" بالمعنى العام، وعلى هذا فلا يخفى النقيض الحقيقي ولازمه في المخصوصات أيضاً.
And as for if it is modal, then the modal is either a mode of necessity or possibility. If it is a mode of necessity, then it is either absolute (unconditioned) or conditioned. If it is absolute, then the proposition is either universal, particular, or specific. If it is universal, then either affirmative or negative. If it is affirmative, like our saying 'necessarily every B is J', then its real contradictory is the removal of its predicate from its subject in respect of what it is modal, without paying attention to whether what is true is the removal of the affirmative relation along with the mode or the mode only. For from the necessity of the falsity in establishing the predicate for the subject with a mode, the truth of its negation in respect of what it established, and that is to say 'not necessarily every B is J'. And in this way is the real contradictory for every modal proposition, whether the proposition is quantified or singular, affirmative or negative, and the mode is necessary or possible in any consideration. And the necessary consequent of 'not necessarily every B is J' is 'by general possibility, not every B is J'. That it is particular negative is necessary because it is the contradictory of the universal affirmative as was known. And that it is by general possibility is because it is permissible that the negation in some is by necessity or by general possibility, for both are indeed general possibility. And on this, if it is universal negative, like our saying 'necessarily no B is J', then the necessary consequent of its contradictory is 'by general possibility, some B is J', according to what we established. And if it is particular, then either affirmative or negative. If it is affirmative, like our saying 'necessarily some B is J', then its real contradictory is 'not necessarily some B is J', and its consequent is 'it is possible that no B is J in the general sense'. And on this, the real contradictory and its consequent in the specific propositions are not hidden.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage introduces modal propositions (al-muwajjaha) and explains how to determine their real contradictories (al-naqīḍ al-ḥaqīqī) and their necessary consequents (lāzim). The author first categorizes modal propositions by mode: necessity (ḍarūra) and possibility (imkān). For necessity, it can be absolute (muṭlaqa, unconditioned) or conditioned (mashrūṭa). Focusing on absolute necessity, the author considers universal, particular, and specific propositions. For the universal affirmative 'necessarily every B is J', the real contradictory is 'not necessarily every B is J'—this negates the mode, not just the predicate. The necessary consequent of that contradictory is 'by general possibility, some B is not J'. The justification is that the contradictory must be a particular negative, and the mode of possibility covers both necessary and possible negations. Similarly, for 'necessarily no B is J', the real contradictory's consequent is 'by general possibility, some B is J'. For the particular affirmative 'necessarily some B is J', the contradictory is 'not necessarily some B is J', and its consequent is 'it is possible that no B is J in the general sense'. The author concludes that the same methodology extends to specific propositions.

Finding the True Contradictory of Modal Propositions
Author explains how to find the true contradictory and its consequent for quantified modal propositions of necessity and possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the true contradictory and its necessary consequent for any modal categorical proposition (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative) using the rules of quantity, quality, and modality.

Core Idea
The true contradictory flips quantity, quality, and changes necessity to general possibility.

Modal propositions, such as 'necessarily every B is C', carry a modality of necessity or possibility. To find the proposition that directly contradicts them—the true contradictory—you must change both quantity (universal to particular or vice versa) and quality (affirmative to negative), and also adjust the modality: necessity becomes general possibility. This lesson walks through the method for each type of quantified modal proposition, using examples from Islamic legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition's type
Determine whether it is universal affirmative (e.g., 'necessarily every B is C'), universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative, and note the modality (necessary or possible).
2
Flip quantity and quality
For the true contradictory, change universal to particular, affirmative to negative (or vice versa). The contradictory of a universal affirmative is a particular negative, and so on.
3
Adjust the modality
If the original is necessary, the contradictory uses general possibility (imkan 'amm). If the original is possible, the contradictory uses necessity? The passage only explicitly treats necessity; for possibility, the pattern is symmetrical: the contradictory of a necessary proposition is possible, and vice versa.
General possibility means 'not impossible'—the weakest sense of possibility.
4
Determine the consequent (lazim)
From the contradictory, you can infer a further proposition that necessarily follows. For a universal affirmative, the consequent is 'by general possibility, some B is not C' (i.e., the contradictory itself is that form). For a universal negative, the consequent is 'by general possibility, some B is C'. For particular affirmatives, the consequent is 'possible that no B is C'.
5
Apply to singular propositions
Singular propositions (e.g., 'Zayd is a writer necessarily') follow the same pattern: the contradictory is 'Not necessarily Zayd is a writer' (equivalent to 'Possibly Zayd is not a writer'), and the consequent is the same.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى هذا النحو يكون النقيض الحقيقي لكل قضية موجهة كانت القضية محصورة أو شخصية موجبة أو سالبة والجهة ضرورية أو ممكنة بكل اعتبار، ولازم ليس بالضرورة كل ب ج، وبالإمكان العام ليس بعض ب ج. أما أنه جزئي سالب فلضرورة أنه نقيض الكلي الموجبة كما عرف، وإما أنه بالإمكان العام، فلجواز أن يكون السلب في البعض بالضرورة أو الإمكان العام لهما إنما هو الممكن العام. وعلى هذا إن كانت كلية سالبة كقولنا: بالضرورة لا شيء من ب ج، فلازم نقيضها بالإمكان العام بعض ب ج لما حققناه. وإن كانت جزئية فإما موجبة وإما سالبة، فإن كانت موجبة كقولنا بالضرورة بعض ب ج، فنقيضها الحقيقي ليس بالضرورة بعض ب ج ولازمه ممكن أن لا يكون شيء من ب ج بالمعنى العام. وعلى هذا فلا يخفى النقيض الحقيقي ولازمه في المخصوصات أيضاً.
And in this manner is the true contradictory for every modal proposition, whether the proposition is quantified or singular, affirmative or negative, and the modality is necessary or possible in every consideration. And its consequent is 'not necessarily every B is C' and 'by general possibility, not some B is C'. As for it being particular negative, it is necessary because it is the contradictory of the universal affirmative, as known. And as for it being by general possibility, because it is possible that the negation is in some [cases] by necessity or by general possibility; the two together are only general possibility. And on this, if [the proposition] is a universal negative, like our saying: 'necessarily, no B is C', then the consequent of its contradictory is 'by general possibility, some B is C' according to what we have established. And if it is particular, then either affirmative or negative. If it is affirmative, like our saying: 'necessarily, some B is C', then its true contradictory is 'not necessarily some B is C', and its consequent is 'it is possible that no B is C' in the general sense. And on this, the true contradictory and its consequent in singular propositions are not hidden.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage defines the method for finding the true contradictory (al-naqid al-haqiqi) and its necessary consequent (al-lazim) for modal categorical propositions. The author considers propositions that are either quantified (universal or particular) or singular, with modalities of necessity (darura) or possibility (imkan). The key principle is that the true contradictory must differ in both quantity and quality: a universal affirmative's contradictory is a particular negative, and vice versa. Additionally, the modality flips in a specific way: if the original is necessary, the contradictory is possible in the general sense (bi-al-imkan al-amm). For example, if we have 'necessarily, every B is C', its true contradictory is 'not necessarily every B is C' (which is equivalent to 'possibly some B is not C'), and its consequent (a further immediate inference) is 'by general possibility, some B is not C'. The author then applies this to universal negatives and particulars, showing that the pattern holds. The passage also notes that for singular propositions, the same method applies without complication. This discussion is foundational for understanding how modal propositions relate logically.

Key Terms
النقيض الحقيقي
اللازم
الإمكان العام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the true contradictory of 'بالضرورة كل ب ج' (necessarily every B is C)?
A) بالضرورة ليس كل ب ج (necessarily not every B is C)
B) بالضرورة لا شيء من ب ج (necessarily no B is C)
C) ليس بالضرورة كل ب ج (not necessarily every B is C) which is equivalent to بالإمكان العام ليس بعض ب ج (by general possibility, some B is not C)
D) بالإمكان العام كل ب ج (by general possibility, every B is C)

Key Takeaway
To find the true contradictory of a modal proposition, flip the quantity and quality, and change necessity to general possibility (or vice versa).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the proposition 'بالضرورة كل خمر حرام' (necessarily every wine is forbidden). Identify its true contradictory and the consequent as described in the passage. Then do the same for 'بالضرورة لا شيء من الخمر حلال' (necessarily no wine is permitted).
?
Construct the true contradictory and consequent for the singular proposition 'زيد كاتب بالضرورة' (Zayd is a writer necessarily) using the method from the passage.

Contradictory of Conditional Necessity: Permanent Subject Condition
Author derives the true contradictory for conditional necessity with permanent subject condition and permanent predicate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the true contradictory and necessary consequent for a universal affirmative conditional necessity proposition where the condition is permanent by the permanence of the subject.

Core Idea
The falsehood of a conditional necessity proposition can be in the whole or in the part; the contradictory must capture all possibilities without altering the condition.

In this lesson, we focus on the first case from the text: conditional necessity where the condition is tied to the subject's permanence. The author walks through constructing the true contradictory for the universal affirmative when the predicate is also permanent. This is the foundation for handling more complex modal propositions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition type
Determine that the necessity is conditional and the condition is 'permanent by the permanence of the subject' (i.e., the subject being B). The proposition is of the form: 'Everything that is B permanently by the permanence of the subject is C.'
This is the first subcase from the passage.
2
Check if the predicate is permanent
Assume the predicate is also permanent by the permanence of the condition. The proposition becomes: 'Every B is C permanently as long as B exists.'
The author explicitly treats this case first.
3
Analyze possibilities of falsehood
The proposition can be false in three ways: (i) false in all instances (universal negative true), (ii) false in some instances (particular negative true), or (iii) false in some instances because the negation is true in some times but not all times.
This step follows the author's reasoning: 'possible that the falsehood is in the whole, possible that it is in the part'.
4
Construct the true contradictory
The contradictory must negate the original while preserving the condition. The resulting form is: 'Not everything that is B permanently by the permanence of the subject is C in some times of its being B.' This is a particular negative with a temporal qualifier.
This matches the author's conclusion: 'the consequent is: not everything that is B... is C in some times of its being B'.
5
Derive the necessary consequent
From the contradictory, the author extracts a necessary consequent (lazim) that must hold. In this case, it is 'some B is not C in some time of being B' (i.e., a particular negative with temporal restriction).
The consequent is not the contradictory itself but a logical implication of it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كانت الضرورة مشروطة فإما أن يكون شرطها هو شرط الموضوع أو المحمول أو الوقت. فإن كان شرط الموضوع فإما أن يكون دائماً بدوام الموضوع أو غير دائم بدوامه. فإن كان الأول فإما أن يكون المحمول دائماً بدوامه أو غير دائم بدوامه. فإن كان المحمول دائماً بدوامه فالقضية إما أن تكون محصورة أو مخصوصة. فإن كانت محصورة فإما كلية وإما جزئية. فإن كانت كلية فإما موجبة وإما سالبة. فإن كانت موجبة كقولنا: (كل ما هو ب دائماً بدوام الموضوع فهو ج) ما دام الموضوع متصفاً بـ ب. ولما احتمل أن يكون الكذب في الكل، واحتمل أن يكون في البعض، واحتمل أن يكون الكذب في البعض لصدق السلب الدائم ما دام ب وصدق سلبه في بعض أوقات كونه ب لا ما دام ب، كان اللازم ليس كل ما هو ب دائماً بدوام الموضوع ج في بعض أوقات كونه ب. وعليك باعتبار السالبة بنفسك. وعلى هذا إن كانت القضية جزئية أو شخصية فقد تمهد لك كيفية اعتبار نقيضها الحقيقي ولازمه فاعتبر. وإن كان المحمول غير دائم بدوام الشرط بل هو للموضع في بعض أوقات كونه متصفاً بالشرط فالقضية أيضاً إما مخصوصة أو محصورة. فإن كانت محصورة فإما كلية وإما جزئية. فإن كانت كلية فإما موجبة أو سالبة. فإن كانت موجبة كقولنا: (كل ما هو ب دائم بدوام الموضوع فهو ج لا دائماً ما دام ب بل في بعض أوقات كونه ب). فنقيضها الحقيقي ليس كل ما هو ب دائماً بدوام الموضوع فهو ج لا دائماً ما دام ب بل في بعض أوقات كونه ب. ولما كان الكذب قد يكون في الكل وقد يكون في البعض كان الكذب في البعض متيقناً لما عرف ثم الكذب في ذلك البعض إما أن يكون لقولنا: (هو ج في بعض أوقات كونه ب) وإما لقولنا لا دائماً. فإن كان الأول صدق السلب الدائم، وإن كان الثاني صدق الإيجاب الدائم. فكان اللازم إما بعض ما هو دائم ب ليس ج ما دام ب، وأما بعض ما هو دائماً دائماً ب فهو ج ما دام ب. وعليك باعتبار النقيض الحقيقي ولازمه في ما بقي من القضايا المحصورة والمخصوصة. وأما إن كان الشرط غير دائم بدوام الموضوع، فإما أن يكون المحمول دائماً بدوامه أو غير دائم بدوامه بل هو للموضوع في بعض أوقات كونه متصفاً بالشرط. فإن كان الأول فالقضية إما دائماً بل ما دام ب. ولما احتمل أن يكون الكذب في الكل، واحتمل أن يكون في البعض، والكذب في البعض متيقن، كان اللازم جزئياً. ولما احتمل أن يكون الكذب في البعض لقولنا: (لا دائماً) صدق الإيجاب الدائم، أو لقولنا: (ما دام ب) واحتمل أن يكون ذلك لصدق السلب في جميع أوقات كونه ب، واحتمل أن يكون في بعض أوقات كونه ب وكان البعض أيضاً متيقناً كان اللازم إما بعض ما هو ب لا دائما بدوام الموضوع فهو ج دائماً) وإما (بعض ما ب لا دائماً بدوام الموضوع فهو ج دائماً) وإما (ليس ج في بعض أوقات كونه ب). وعلى هذا فلا يخفى اعتبار النقيض ولازمه في ما بقي من القضايا المحصورة والمخصوصة.
And if the necessity is conditional, then its condition is either the condition of the subject, the predicate, or the time. If it is the condition of the subject, then it is either permanent by the permanence of the subject or not permanent by its permanence. If the first, then the predicate is either permanent by its permanence or not permanent by its permanence. If the predicate is permanent by its permanence, then the proposition is either quantified or singular. If quantified, then either universal or particular. If universal, then either affirmative or negative. If affirmative, like our saying: 'Everything that is B permanently by the permanence of the subject is C' as long as the subject is qualified by B. And since it is possible that the falsehood is in the whole, and possible that it is in the part, and possible that the falsehood is in the part due to the truth of the permanent negation as long as B and the truth of its negation in some times of its being B not as long as B, the consequent is 'not everything that is B permanently by the permanence of the subject is C in some times of its being B'. And you must consider the negative by yourself. And on this, if the proposition is particular or singular, the manner of considering its true contradictory and its consequent has been prepared for you, so consider. And if the predicate is not permanent by the permanence of the condition but rather it is for the subject in some times of its being qualified by the condition, then the proposition is also either singular or quantified. If quantified, then either universal or particular. If universal, then either affirmative or negative. If affirmative, like our saying: 'Everything that is B permanent by the permanence of the subject is C not always as long as B but in some times of its being B'. Its true contradictory is 'not everything that is B permanent by the permanence of the subject is C not always as long as B but in some times of its being B'. And since falsehood may be in the whole and may be in the part, the falsehood in the part is certain as known. Then the falsehood in that part is either due to our saying: 'it is C in some times of its being B' or due to our saying 'not always'. If the first, the permanent negation is true; if the second, the permanent affirmation is true. So the consequent is either 'some of what is permanently B is not C as long as B' or 'some of what is permanently B is C as long as B'. And you must consider the true contradictory and its consequent in the rest of quantified and singular propositions. And if the condition is not permanent by the permanence of the subject, then either the predicate is permanent by its permanence or not permanent by its permanence, but rather it is for the subject in some times of its being qualified by the condition. If the first, then the proposition is either quantified or singular. If quantified, then if it is a universal affirmative, as in our saying: 'Everything that is B not permanently by the permanence of the subject is C not always but as long as B'. Its true contradictory is 'not everything that is B not permanently by the permanence of the subject is C not always but as long as B'. And since it is possible that the falsehood is in the whole, and possible that it is in the part, and the falsehood in the part is certain, the consequent is particular. And since it is possible that the falsehood in the part is due to our saying: 'not always' [making] the permanent affirmation true, or due to our saying: 'as long as B' and it is possible that that is due to the truth of the negation in all times of its being B, and possible that it is in some times of its being B, and the part is also certain, the consequent is either 'some of what is B not permanently by the permanence of the subject is C permanently' or 'some of what is B not permanently by the permanence of the subject is C permanently' or 'not C in some times of its being B'. And on this, the consideration of the contradictory and its consequent in the rest of quantified and singular propositions is not hidden.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage extends the analysis of modal contradictories to propositions with conditional necessity (al-darura al-mashruta). The author systematically classifies the types of conditions: the condition may be about the subject, the predicate, or the time. Focusing on the case where the condition is about the subject, he further divides based on whether the condition is permanent with the subject's permanence or not, and similarly whether the predicate is permanent. For each combination, he provides an example of a universal affirmative and constructs its true contradictory and consequent. The method is analogous to the simple modal case but more complex due to the additional condition. The author often leaves the negative case for the reader to work out ('consider by yourself'), indicating that this is a pedagogical exercise. The passage is highly technical and aims to equip the student with the ability to analyze any conditional modal proposition into its contradictory and necessary consequent.

Given the conditional necessity universal affirmative: 'Everything that is created permanently by its creation is contingent (as long as it is created)', what is its true contradictory?
A) Not everything that is created permanently by its creation is contingent in some times of its being created.

Contradictory of Conditional Necessity: Non-Permanent Subject Condition
Author derives the contradictory and consequents for conditional necessity with non-permanent subject condition and permanent predicate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the true contradictory and its possible consequents for a conditional necessity universal affirmative where the condition is not permanent by the permanence of the subject.

Core Idea
When the condition is not permanent, falsehood can arise from either the affirmation or negation of the predicate, leading to multiple possible consequents.

The second major case in the author's analysis deals with conditional necessity where the condition is not permanent (i.e., the subject is not always permanently qualified). This introduces more complexity, as the falsehood can be due to either the permanent affirmation or permanent negation of the predicate. The author shows how to derive the contradictory and the two possible necessary consequents.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition as non-permanent
The condition is 'not permanent by the permanence of the subject'. The proposition is: 'Everything that is B not permanently by the permanence of the subject is C not always but as long as B.' This means the predicate holds only during the times the subject is B, but the subject is not always permanently B.
This is the second main branch after the permanent condition.
2
Determine predicate permanence
Assume the predicate is permanent by its permanence (i.e., it holds whenever the subject is B). The proposition becomes: 'Every B (not permanently) is C permanently as long as B.'
The author treats this as the first subcase under non-permanent condition.
3
Construct the true contradictory
The contradictory of the universal affirmative with non-permanent condition is: 'Not everything that is B not permanently by the permanence of the subject is C permanently as long as B.' This negates the universal quantification while keeping the condition.
The author states: 'the true contradictory is not everything... not permanently... etc.'
4
Analyze falsehood possibilities
Falsehood can be in the whole or in the part. Since falsehood in the part is certain, the contradictory yields a particular consequent. The falsehood in the part can be due to either: (i) the predicate not holding always (negation true in some times), or (ii) the predicate holding always (affirmation true in all times). This gives two possible consequents.
The author says: 'if the first, the permanent negation is true; if the second, the permanent affirmation is true.'
5
Derive the two possible consequents
The two consequents are: (1) 'Some of what is B not permanently is C permanently as long as B' (from permanent affirmation being true) and (2) 'Some of what is B not permanently is not C permanently as long as B' (from permanent negation being true). Actually the passage gives: 'some of what is B not permanently... is C permanently' OR 'some... is not C...'. The author presents them as alternatives.
The consequents are not unique; the contradictory forces one of them to be true, but we cannot determine which.

الأول صدق السلب الدائم، وإن كان الثاني صدق الإيجاب الدائم. فكان اللازم إما بعض ما هو دائم ب ليس ج ما دام ب، وأما بعض ما هو دائماً دائماً ب فهو ج ما دام ب. وعليك باعتبار النقيض الحقيقي ولازمه في ما بقي محصورة أو مخصوصة. فإن كانت محصورة فإن كانت كلية موجبة كما في قولنا: (كل ما هو ب لا دائماً بدوام الموضوع فهو ج لا دائماً بل ما دام ب). فنقيضها الحقيقي ليس كل ما هو ب لا دائماً بدوام الموضوع فهو ج لا دائماً بل ما دام ب. ولما احتمل أن يكون الكذب في الكل، واحتمل أن يكون في البعض، والكذب في البعض متيقن، كان اللازم جزئياً. ولما احتمل أن يكون الكذب في البعض لقولنا: (لا دائماً) صدق الإيجاب الدائم، أو لقولنا: (ما دام ب) واحتمل أن يكون ذلك لصدق السلب في جميع أوقات كونه ب، واحتمل أن يكون في بعض أوقات كونه ب وكان البعض أيضاً متيقناً كان اللازم إما بعض ما هو ب لا دائما بدوام الموضوع فهو ج دائماً) وإما (بعض ما ب لا دائماً بدوام الموضوع فهو ج دائماً) وإما (ليس ج في بعض أوقات كونه ب). وعلى هذا فلا يخفى اعتبار النقيض ولازمه في ما بقي من القضايا المحصورة والمخصوصة.
And if the necessity is conditional, then its condition is either the condition of the subject, the predicate, or the time. If it is the condition of the subject, then it is either permanent by the permanence of the subject or not permanent by its permanence. If the first, then the predicate is either permanent by its permanence or not permanent by its permanence. If the predicate is permanent by its permanence, then the proposition is either quantified or singular. If quantified, then either universal or particular. If universal, then either affirmative or negative. If affirmative, like our saying: 'Everything that is B permanently by the permanence of the subject is C' as long as the subject is qualified by B. And since it is possible that the falsehood is in the whole, and possible that it is in the part, and possible that the falsehood is in the part due to the truth of the permanent negation as long as B and the truth of its negation in some times of its being B not as long as B, the consequent is 'not everything that is B permanently by the permanence of the subject is C in some times of its being B'. And you must consider the negative by yourself. And on this, if the proposition is particular or singular, the manner of considering its true contradictory and its consequent has been prepared for you, so consider. And if the predicate is not permanent by the permanence of the condition but rather it is for the subject in some times of its being qualified by the condition, then the proposition is also either singular or quantified. If quantified, then either universal or particular. If universal, then either affirmative or negative. If affirmative, like our saying: 'Everything that is B permanent by the permanence of the subject is C not always as long as B but in some times of its being B'. Its true contradictory is 'not everything that is B permanent by the permanence of the subject is C not always as long as B but in some times of its being B'. And since falsehood may be in the whole and may be in the part, the falsehood in the part is certain as known. Then the falsehood in that part is either due to our saying: 'it is C in some times of its being B' or due to our saying 'not always'. If the first, the permanent negation is true; if the second, the permanent affirmation is true. So the consequent is either 'some of what is permanently B is not C as long as B' or 'some of what is permanently B is C as long as B'. And you must consider the true contradictory and its consequent in the rest of quantified and singular propositions. And if the condition is not permanent by the permanence of the subject, then either the predicate is permanent by its permanence or not permanent by its permanence, but rather it is for the subject in some times of its being qualified by the condition. If the first, then the proposition is either quantified or singular. If quantified, then if it is a universal affirmative, as in our saying: 'Everything that is B not permanently by the permanence of the subject is C not always but as long as B'. Its true contradictory is 'not everything that is B not permanently by the permanence of the subject is C not always but as long as B'. And since it is possible that the falsehood is in the whole, and possible that it is in the part, and the falsehood in the part is certain, the consequent is particular. And since it is possible that the falsehood in the part is due to our saying: 'not always' [making] the permanent affirmation true, or due to our saying: 'as long as B' and it is possible that that is due to the truth of the negation in all times of its being B, and possible that it is in some times of its being B, and the part is also certain, the consequent is either 'some of what is B not permanently by the permanence of the subject is C permanently' or 'some of what is B not permanently by the permanence of the subject is C permanently' or 'not C in some times of its being B'. And on this, the consideration of the contradictory and its consequent in the rest of quantified and singular propositions is not hidden.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage extends the analysis of modal contradictories to propositions with conditional necessity (al-darura al-mashruta). The author systematically classifies the types of conditions: the condition may be about the subject, the predicate, or the time. Focusing on the case where the condition is about the subject, he further divides based on whether the condition is permanent with the subject's permanence or not, and similarly whether the predicate is permanent. For each combination, he provides an example of a universal affirmative and constructs its true contradictory and consequent. The method is analogous to the simple modal case but more complex due to the additional condition. The author often leaves the negative case for the reader to work out ('consider by yourself'), indicating that this is a pedagogical exercise. The passage is highly technical and aims to equip the student with the ability to analyze any conditional modal proposition into its contradictory and necessary consequent.

Key Terms
الضرورة المشروطة
دائم بدوام الموضوع
اللازم

Real Negation of Universal Affirmative Conditional with Permanent Condition
Definition and analysis of real negation for universal affirmative conditional with permanent condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the real negation of a universal affirmative conditional proposition with a permanent condition and derive its necessary consequences.

Core Idea
The real negation of a universal conditional is not simply a quantifier change; it requires analyzing the temporal condition and the distribution of falsehood.

In this passage, the author explains how to form the real negation (al-naqid al-haqiqi) of a universal affirmative conditional where the condition is permanent (da'im) with the subject. This is a critical skill in Islamic logic for evaluating truth conditions of complex quantified propositions used in theological and juristic arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Determine if the proposition is quantified (mahsuura) or particular (makhsusa). If quantified, is it universal or particular? If universal, is it affirmative or negative?
The passage begins by classifying propositions with a condition.
2
Parse the example
Take the given example: 'Every B that is permanently (due to the permanence of the subject) is J, not always as long as it is B, but in some times of its being B.' Understand the subject term (B with permanent condition) and predicate term (J with temporal qualifier).
The variables 'ب' (B) and 'ج' (J) represent arbitrary terms.
3
Form the real negation
Prefix 'ليس كل' (not every) to the entire proposition, keeping the rest identical. This yields the real negation: 'Not every B that is permanently ... is J, not always ...'.
This is the direct contradictory, not merely a particular negative.
4
Analyze possibilities of falsehood
Since the original is false, the falsehood may be in the whole or in a part. For a universal, falsehood in a part is certain. That part is either due to the affirmative component 'it is J in some times' or the negative component 'not always'.
The author assumes the universal affirmative is false, which triggers the analysis.
5
Derive necessary consequences
If falsehood is due to the affirmative part, then the permanent negative 'some B is not J as long as it is B' is true. If due to the negative part, then the permanent affirmative 'some B is J as long as it is B' is true. These are the possible necessary consequences (lazim) of the real negation.
The 'lazim' are not the negation itself but entailments of its truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كونه متصفاً بالشرط فالقضية أيضاً إما مخصوصة أو محصورة، فإن كانت محصورة فإما كليّة وإما جزئية، فإن كانت كليّة فإما موجبة أو سالبة، فإن كانت موجبة كقولنا: (كل ما هو "ب" دائم بدوام الموضوع فهو "ج" لا دائماً ما دام "ب" بل في بعض أوقات كونه "ب"، فنقيضها الحقيقي ليس كل ما هو "ب" دائماً بدوام الموضوع فهو "ج" لا دائماً ما دام "ب" بل في بعض أوقات كونه "ب")، ولما كان الكذب قد يكون في الكل وقد يكون في البعض كان الكذب في البعض متيقناً لما عرف ثم الكذب في ذلك البعض إما أن يكون لقولنا: (هو "ج" في بعض أوقات كونه "ب") وإما لقولنا لا دائماً)، فإن كان الأول صدق السلب الدائم، وإن كان الثاني صدق الإيجاب الدائم، فكان اللازم إما بعض ما هو دائم "ب" ليس "ج" ما دام "ب"، وأما بعض ما هو دائماً دائماً "ب" فهو "ج" ما دام "ب" وعليك باعتبار النقيض الحقيقي ولازمه في ما بقي من القضايا المحصورة والمخصوصة.
When it is qualified by the condition, the proposition is either particular or quantified. If quantified, it is either universal or particular. If universal, it is either affirmative or negative. If affirmative, as in our saying: 'Every B that is permanently (due to the permanence of the subject) is J, not always as long as it is B, but in some times of its being B,' then its real negation is 'Not every B is permanently (due to the permanence of the subject) J, not always as long as it is B, but in some times of its being B.' And since falsehood may be in the whole or in the part, falsehood in the part is certain, as known. Then falsehood in that part is either for our saying: 'It is J in some times of its being B' or for our saying 'not always.' If the first, then the permanent negative is true; if the second, then the permanent affirmative is true. So the necessary consequence is either some of what is permanently B is not J as long as it is B, or some of what is permanently B is J as long as it is B. And you must consider the real negation and its necessary consequence in the remaining quantified and particular propositions.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage defines how to form the real negation (al-naqid al-haqiqi) of a universal affirmative conditional proposition where the condition is permanent (da'im) with the subject's permanence. The author first classifies propositions qualified by a condition: they are either particular (makhsusa) or quantified (mahsuura). Quantified are universal or particular; universal are affirmative or negative. The example given is a universal affirmative: 'Every B that is permanently (due to the permanence of the subject) is J, not always as long as it is B, but in some times of its being B.' The real negation is formed by prefixing 'not every' and keeping the rest. The author then analyzes the possibilities of falsehood: it can be in the whole proposition or in a part. Since the universal affirmative is vulnerable to falsehood in a particular instance, that is certain. The falsehood in that part is either because the predicate is false in some time (making the permanent negative true) or because the temporal qualification 'not always' is false (making the permanent affirmative true). Consequently, the necessary consequence is either a particular negative or a particular affirmative with respect to the condition. The author instructs the reader to apply this method to the remaining types of quantified and particular propositions.

Real Negation of Conditional with Non-Permanent Condition
Author explains real negation for conditionals with non-permanent condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the real negation of a conditional proposition with a non-permanent condition and determine its necessary consequences in terms of particular propositions.

Core Idea
Negating a conditional with a non-permanent condition requires splitting possibilities of falsehood, leading to particular consequences.

We have already seen how to negate simple and conditional propositions. Now we tackle a more complex type: when the condition is not permanent with the subject. This passage from Daqa'iq al-Haqa'iq walks through two subcases, showing how the real negation yields particular propositions with temporal qualifications. Mastering this helps in analyzing arguments where conditions are not always present.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition's permanence
Determine whether the condition (e.g., 'being B') is permanent with the subject's permanence. Here it is not permanent: the subject is not always B.
This is the key feature of the propositions discussed.
2
Determine the predicate's relation to the condition
Check if the predicate (J) is permanent due to the condition (always true when B holds) or only constant in some times. There are two subcases in the passage.
The predicate's permanence affects the resulting consequences.
3
Write the original universal affirmative proposition
Explicitly write the proposition including temporal qualifications: e.g., 'Every B that is not permanently due to the permanence of the subject is J, not always but as long as it is B.'
Ensure the temporal parts are clear.
4
Form the real negation
Prefix 'not every' (laysa kull) to the proposition, keeping the rest exactly the same. This gives the contradictory.
The negation is a particular proposition because the original was universal.
5
Analyze the two possibilities of falsehood
Consider that the falsehood of the whole could be due to either the 'not always' part being false (making a permanent affirmative true) or the 'as long as it is B' part being false (making a negative true in all or some times).
This yields the necessary consequences, which are particular propositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
