And if the condition is not permanent due to the permanence of the subject, then either the predicate is permanent due to its permanence or not permanent due to its permanence but rather belongs to the subject in some times of its being qualified by the condition. If the first, then the proposition is either quantified or particular. If quantified, then if it is a universal affirmative as in our saying: 'Every B that is not permanently due to the permanence of the subject is J, not always but as long as it is B,' then its real negation is 'Not every B that is not permanently due to the permanence of the subject is J, not always but as long as it is B.' And since it is possible that falsehood is in the whole and possible that it is in the part, and falsehood in the part is certain, the necessary consequence is particular. And since it is possible that falsehood in the part is due to our saying '(not) always' – the truth of the permanent affirmative, or due to our saying 'as long as it is B' – and it is possible that it is due to the truth of the negation in all times of its being B, and possible that it is in some times of its being B, and the part is also certain, then the necessary consequence is either 'some of what is B, not permanently due to the permanence of the subject, is J permanently' or 'some of what is B, not permanently due to the permanence of the subject, is J permanently' or 'it is not J in some times of its being B.' If quantified, then if it is a universal affirmative as in our saying: 'Every B that is not permanently due to the permanence of the subject is J, not always but in some times of its being qualified by B' then its real negation is 'Not every B that is not permanently due to the permanence of the subject is J, not always but in some times of its being qualified by B' and the necessary consequence is either 'some of what is B, not permanently due to the permanence of the subject, is J permanently' or 'it is not J nor in any time of its being B.' Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of real negation for conditional propositions, now focusing on cases where the condition is not permanent with the subject's permanence. The author distinguishes two subcases: (1) the predicate is permanent with the subject's permanence, and (2) the predicate is constant (thabit) in some times. For the first subcase, he gives the example of a universal affirmative: 'Every B that is not permanently due to the permanence of the subject is J, not always but as long as it is B.' The real negation is formed by prefixing 'not every' and keeping the rest. Since falsehood can be in the whole or a part, and falsehood in a part is certain, the necessary consequence is particular. The author then analyzes possible sources of falsehood in the part: either the temporal qualification 'not always' is false (making the permanent affirmative true) or 'as long as it is B' is false (making the negation true in all or some times). The necessary consequences are then either a particular affirmative with 'permanently' or a particular negative in some times. For the second subcase, the example is similar but the predicate is constant only in some times. The real negation and necessary consequences are given: either a particular affirmative with 'permanently' or a particular negative with 'nor in any time.' The author instructs the reader to apply this method to all remaining quantified and particular propositions.

Forming the Real Negation with Predicate Condition
Author defines the real negation for a predicate-condition conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to form the real negation of a universal affirmative conditional where the condition qualifies the predicate.

Core Idea
Negate the quantifier and temporal scope, but keep the condition.

This lesson focuses on the precise method for negating a conditional proposition when the attached condition (shart) belongs to the predicate (mahmul), not the subject. The author provides a concrete example from the universal affirmative. Understanding this distinction is crucial for accurate logical inference in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition and condition type
Start with a universal affirmative proposition: 'Every B is J always, but as long as it is J.' The condition 'as long as it is J' qualifies the predicate J, not the subject B.
This is a 'condition of the predicate' (shart al-mahmul).
2
Form the real negation
Replace the universal quantifier 'Every' with 'Not every', and negate the temporal part: 'always' becomes 'not always'. The condition itself remains unchanged. So the negation is: 'Not every B is J, not always but as long as it is J.'
The condition is retained because it belongs to the predicate.
3
Verify with the author's example
Compare your result with the author's wording: 'ليس كل ب ج لا دائماً بل ما دام ج'. Confirm that the structure matches: 'Not every... not always... but as long as...'
The author explicitly states this as the real negation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان الشرط هو شرط المحمول كقولنا في الكلية الموجبة (كل) "ب" "ج" دائماً بل ما دام "ج") فنقيضه الحقيقي ليس كل "ب" "ج" لا دائماً بل ما دام "ج")، واللازم إما (بعض "ب" "ج" دائماً) وإما (بعض "ب" "ج" دائماً) وإما (بعض "ب" ليس "ج" في بعض أوقات كونه متصفاً بـ "ج") لا يكون حقاً، وعليك باعتبار النقيض الحقيقي ولازمه في ما بقي من القضايا.
And if the condition is the condition of the predicate, as in our saying in the universal affirmative: 'Every B is J always, but as long as it is J,' then its real negation is 'Not every B is J, not always but as long as it is J,' and the necessary consequence is either 'some B is J always' or 'some B is J always' or 'some B is not J in some times of its being qualified by J' is not true. And you must consider the real negation and its necessary consequence in the remaining propositions.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage addresses the case where the condition (shart) qualifies the predicate (mahmul) rather than the subject. The example given is a universal affirmative: 'Every B is J always, but as long as it is J.' The condition 'as long as it is J' attaches to the predicate J. The real negation is formed by prefixing 'not every' and negating the temporal part: 'Not every B is J, not always but as long as it is J.' The author then lists three possible necessary consequences, though two are identical in the text (likely a scribal duplication). The third is 'some B is not J in some times of its being qualified by J,' which he notes is not true (la yakuna haqqan). This indicates that the necessary consequences are limited to the first two, and the third is not valid. The author again instructs the reader to apply this to all remaining propositions.

Key Terms
شَرْطُ المَحْمُولِ
نقيض حقيقي
كلية موجبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The real negation of a predicate-condition universal affirmative retains the condition but flips the quantifier and negates the temporal scope.

Identifying Necessary Consequences of the Real Negation
Author lists necessary consequences of the real negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which necessary consequences follow from the real negation of a predicate-condition universal affirmative.

Core Idea
Not all subalternates of a negation are valid; some may be false.

After forming the real negation, the author discusses its necessary consequences (lawazim). He lists three possible outcomes, but one is not true. This lesson teaches you to evaluate which consequences are logically entailed and which are not.

Guided Path
1
Write down the real negation
From the previous lesson, the real negation is 'Not every B is J, not always but as long as it is J.'
2
Derive the subalternate particular affirmative
The necessary consequence (lazim) is usually the subalternate: 'Some B is J always' (particular affirmative with 'always'). The author lists this as the first two (possibly a scribal duplication).
This follows from the negation because 'Not every B is J' is equivalent to 'Some B is not J', but here the condition complicates.
3
Check the third possible consequence
The author also mentions 'some B is not J in some times of its being qualified by J' and declares it 'not true' (la yakuna haqqan). This is because the condition 'as long as it is J' restricts the time; the negation does not entail that there is a time when B is J but not J? Actually, the passage: 'some B is not J in some times of its being qualified by J' seems contradictory. The author says it is not a valid consequence.
The duplication in the text might indicate only two distinct consequences: the first two are the same, so effectively only one valid consequence.
4
Summarize valid consequences
The only valid necessary consequence is 'Some B is J always.' The third is invalid. The author implies that for other propositions, similar analysis is required.
This teaches precision: not every subalternate of a conditional negation is automatically true.

Commentary

The real negation is formed by prefixing 'not every' and negating the temporal part: 'Not every B is J, not always but as long as it is J.' The author then lists three possible necessary consequences, though two are identical in the text (likely a scribal duplication). The third is 'some B is not J in some times of its being qualified by J,' which he notes is not true (la yakuna haqqan). This indicates that the necessary consequences are limited to the first two, and the third is not valid. The author again instructs the reader to apply this to all remaining propositions.

Real Negation of Time-Conditioned Propositions
Author defines real negation for time-conditioned propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the real negation of any temporal conditional proposition in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Real negation attacks the predication at that time, not other times.

Time conditions often appear in Islamic legal and theological propositions. To properly negate such statements, we must pin the negation to the specified time and not shift to other moments. This lesson unpacks that rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the time condition
Find the temporal term (e.g., 'at noon', 'in Ramadan') that qualifies the predication. The condition is the time in which the predicate applies to the subject.
The time may be definite (mu'ayyan) or indefinite (ghayr mu'ayyan).
2
Determine the predication at that time
Check whether the proposition affirms the predicate of the subject (e.g., 'Zayd is standing at noon') or negates it (e.g., 'Zayd is not standing at noon').
3
Insert the negation particle on the predication
If the original proposition affirms the predicate, the real negation negates it exactly at that time (e.g., 'Zayd is not standing at noon'). If it negates, the real negation affirms it at that time (e.g., 'Zayd is standing at noon').
The negation particle (harf al-salb) must target the same time.
4
Consider quantity and other times
For quantified propositions (e.g., 'All men are standing at noon'), the negation must also change quantity (e.g., 'Some men are not standing at noon'). Additionally, one must be careful not to confuse negation at that time with assertions about other times.
The author notes that affirming what was affirmed in other times is a separate operation, not the real negation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان الشرط هو الوقت ونسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع فيه لا في غيره فسواء كان الوقت معيناً أو غير معين، فالنقيض الحقيقي للقضية المشروطة به إنما يكون بأن يدخل حرف السلب فيها على ما أثبت من حيث ما أثبت أو نفيه من حيث ما نفي، وهي إما أن يكون الكذب فيها لنفس النسبة في ذلك الوقت لما أثبت فيه أو الإيجاب في ذلك الوقت لما نفي فيه، وأما إيجاب ما أثبت فيه في غيره من الأوقات وسلب ما سلب فيه في غيره من الأوقات مع اختلاف كمية القضية إن كانت محصورة.
And if the condition is time, and the relation of the predicate to the subject is in it and not in other than it, whether the time is definite or indefinite, then the real negation of the proposition conditioned by it is only by inserting the negation particle into it concerning what it affirmed in terms of what it affirmed, or its negation in terms of what it negated. And it is either that the falsehood in it is due to the relation itself in that time concerning what was affirmed in it, or the affirmation in that time concerning what was negated in it. And as for affirming what was affirmed in it in other times and negating what was negated in it in other times, with the change of quantity of the proposition if it was quantified.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage covers propositions where the condition is time (wakt), i.e., the predicate is attributed to the subject at a specific time and not at other times. The time can be definite (mu'ayyan) or indefinite (ghayr mu'ayyan). The real negation is formed by inserting the negation particle (harf al-salb) into the proposition, specifically negating what was affirmed or affirming what was negated regarding that time. The falsehood of such a proposition can arise either because the relation itself at that time is false (if the proposition affirmed it) or because the affirmation at that time is false (if the proposition negated it). Additionally, one must consider affirming what was affirmed at that time in other times, or negating what was negated at that time in other times, with the appropriate change in quantity if the proposition is quantified. This shows that the negation of a time-conditioned proposition is not simply the contradictory but involves careful analysis of the temporal scope.

Key Terms
الشرط هو الوقت
النقيض الحقيقي
حرف السلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Zayd is standing at some other time.
B) Zayd is not standing at noon.
C) Zayd is not standing at any time.
D) Zayd is sitting at noon.

Key Takeaway
The real negation of a time-conditioned proposition always negates (or affirms) the predicate at that specific time, not at other times.

Real Negation of General Possibility Propositions
Author begins explaining the real negation for propositions with the modality of possibility, specifically the general sense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the real negation of a universal affirmative modal proposition with general possibility.

Core Idea
Negating a possibility does not yield possibility—it yields necessity.

Modal propositions with the modality of possibility (imkan) have three senses. Here we focus on the general sense (al-imkan al-amm). The real negation of a universal affirmative proposition in this modality is not simply an opposite modality; it becomes a necessity proposition. Understanding this rule is essential for evaluating arguments in kalam and usul al-fiqh that rely on modal opposition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modality and quantifier
The proposition is 'Every B is J with general possibility.' Note the modality (general possibility) and the quantifier (universal affirmative).
General possibility means what is not impossible, a broad contingency.
2
Apply the real negation pattern
The real negation flips the modality to its contrary. For general possibility, the negation is 'Not with general possibility: every B is J.' This is not a simple negation of the predicate; it negates the modality itself.
The phrase 'Not with general possibility' is equivalent to 'necessarily' in this context, as the author hints with the fragment 'bil-darura'.
3
Generalize to other quantifier types
The author instructs to apply the same reasoning to universal negative and particular propositions. The pattern: the negation operates on the modality, not on the quantifier or copula.
For particular propositions, the real negation would be 'Not with general possibility: some B is J' etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كانت الجهة هي جهة الإمكان فإما أن تكون بالمعنى العام أو الخاص أو الأخص، فإن كان الأول وهو أن تكون بالمعنى العام فالقضية إما محصورة أو مخصوصة، فإن كانت محصورة فهي إما كليّة أو جزئية، فإن كانت كلية فإما موجبة أو سالبة، فإن كانت موجبة كقولنا : ((كل) "ب" فهو "ج" بالإمكان العام فنقيضها الحقيقي ليس بالإمكان العام كل "ب" فهو "ج") وما يلزمه فـ (بالضرورة
And if the modality is the modality of possibility, then it is either in the general sense, the special sense, or the more special sense. If the first, which is in the general sense, then the proposition is either quantified or particular. If quantified, it is either universal or particular. If universal, it is either affirmative or negative. If affirmative, as in our saying: 'Every B is J with general possibility,' then its real negation is 'Not with general possibility: every B is J' and what necessarily follows is 'necessarily...'
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage initiates the discussion of modal propositions with the modality of possibility (imkan). The author distinguishes three senses: general (amm), special (khass), and more special (akhass). He begins with the general sense. For a quantified proposition, if it is a universal affirmative like 'Every B is J with general possibility,' the real negation is 'Not with general possibility: every B is J,' which essentially becomes a necessity proposition (as indicated by the fragment 'necessarily' at the end). The text cuts off, but the pattern suggests that the necessary consequence of negating general possibility is necessity. The author then instructs the reader to apply this to the remaining types of quantified and particular propositions. This passage sets the foundation for understanding modal opposition.

The True Contrary of Subject-Conditioned Universal Affirmative
Author defines true contrary of universal affirmative conditional with subject condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the true contrary of any universal affirmative conditional proposition with a subject condition and identify its necessary logical consequences.

Core Idea
True contradiction of a subject-conditioned universal requires negating the whole, not just the predicate.

In this lesson, we examine how to form the true contrary (al-naqid al-haqiqi) for a type of proposition where the predicate holds only under a condition on the subject. This is a subtle but crucial skill in classical logic, as it preserves the truth conditions of contradiction. The passage from Daqa'iq al-Haqa'iq provides the rule for universal affirmative cases and hints at the general method.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Determine if the proposition is a universal affirmative with a condition attached to the subject. For example: 'Every B that is not always with the duration of the subject is J not always but in some times.'
2
Locate the subject condition
The condition is the phrase describing when the subject is considered, e.g., 'that is not always with the duration of the subject' or 'in some times of its being described with B.'
The condition restricts the scope of the subject term.
3
Form the true contrary
Negate the entire proposition by prefixing 'Not every' (laysa kull) while keeping the condition unchanged. For the example, the true contrary is 'Not every B that is not always with the duration of the subject is J not always but in some times.'
The negation applies to the quantifier and the predicate, not just the predicate alone.
4
Determine the necessary consequent
From the true contrary, two possible logical consequences follow: either 'Some B under the condition is always J' or 'Some B under the condition is never J.' These exhaust the alternatives.
The author states that one of these two consequents must hold.
5
Generalize the method
Apply the same pattern to other quantifiers (particular, negative) and qualities to find true contraries for all subject-conditioned propositions.
The author instructs: 'Consider the true contrary and its consequences for the remaining propositions.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان المحمول ثابتاً للموضوع في بعض أوقات كون الموضوع متصفاً بالشرط فالقضية أيضاً إما محصورة أو مخصوصة، فإن كانت محصورة فإن كانت كلية موجبة كقولنا: (كل ما هو "ب" لا دائماً بدوام الموضوع فهو "ج" لا دائماً بل في بعض أوقات كونه متصفاً بب) فنقيضها الحقيقي ليس كل ما هو "ب" لا دائماً بدوام الموضوع فهو "ج" لا دائماً بل في بعض أوقات كونه متصفاً بب) واللازم إما (بعض ما هو "ب" لا دائماً بدوام الموضوع فهو "ج" دائماً) وإما (ليس "ج" ولا في وقت من أوقات كونه "ب") وعليك باعتبار النقائض الحقيقية ولوازمها في ما بقي من القضايا.
If the predicate is affirmed of the subject in some times of the subject being described with the condition, then the proposition is either determinate or indeterminate. If it is determinate and universal affirmative, like our saying: 'Every B that is not always with the duration of the subject is J not always but in some times of its being described with B,' then its true contrary is 'Not every B that is not always with the duration of the subject is J not always but in some times of its being described with B,' and the necessary consequent is either 'Some B that is not always with the duration of the subject is J always' or 'Some B that is not always with the duration of the subject is not J in any time of its being B.' And you must consider the true contrary and its consequences for the remaining propositions.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage defines the true contrary for a universal affirmative conditional proposition where the condition is attached to the subject. The author explains that when the predicate holds only sometimes for the subject under a condition, the proposition is quantified. For the universal affirmative case, the true contrary is formed by negating the whole proposition: 'Not every B... is J...'. The negation yields two possible consequents: either some such B is always J, or some such B is never J. This logical analysis is crucial for understanding how contradictions work in conditional logic. The author instructs the reader to apply this method to all remaining proposition types.

Key Terms
القضية المحصورة
النقيض الحقيقي
شرط الموضوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Not every B that is not always with the duration of the subject is J not always
C) Some B that is not always with the duration of the subject is not J not always
D) No B that is not always with the duration of the subject is J not always

Key Takeaway
The true contrary of a universal affirmative conditional with subject condition is the negation of the whole compound, not just the predicate.

Forming the True Contrary of Time-Conditioned Propositions
Author explains true contrary for time-conditioned propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a time-conditioned proposition and construct its true contrary by applying the rule of negating within the same time condition.

Core Idea
The true contrary of a time-conditioned proposition requires negation within the same time frame.

In Islamic logic, propositions can be conditioned by time, such as 'Zaid is writing on Friday.' The true contrary of such a proposition must preserve the time condition. This lesson explains how to form that contrary correctly, a skill essential for precise theological and legal argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the time condition in the proposition
Look for a specific or indefinite time that restricts the predicate's applicability. For example, 'on Friday' or 'when he is sitting'. The proposition asserts the predicate only in that time.
2
Determine what is affirmed or negated in the original
The original proposition either affirms or negates the predicate for the subject in that time. Note exactly what is being attributed.
3
Enter the negation particle on the original affirmation/negation
To form the true contrary, place the negation on what was affirmed, or on what was negated, while keeping the time condition unchanged. This yields the contradictory opposite.
For example, if original is 'Zaid is writing on Friday', the true contrary is 'Zaid is not writing on Friday'.
4
Distinguish falsehood due to attribution vs. affirmation
The author notes that falsehood in the time-conditioned proposition can arise either because the attribution itself is false in that time, or because the affirmation/negation is false. Understanding this helps avoid errors.
This aligns with the author's explanation of 'النقيض الحقيقي'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان الشرط هو الوقت ونسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع فيه لا في غيره فسواء كان الوقت معيناً أو غير معين، فالنقيض الحقيقي للقضية المشروطة به إنما يكون بأن يدخل حرف السلب فيها على ما أثبت من حيث ما أثبت أو نفيه من حيث ما نفي، وهي إما أن يكون الكذب فيها لنفس النسبة في ذلك الوقت لما أثبت فيه أو الإيجاب في ذلك الوقت لما نفي فيه، وأما إيجاب ما أثبت فيه في غيره من الأوقات وسلب ما سلب فيه في غيره من الأوقات مع اختلاف كمية القضية إن كانت محصورة
But if the condition is time, and the attribution of the predicate to the subject in it and not in other times, whether the time is definite or indefinite, then the true contrary of the proposition conditioned by it is formed by entering the negation particle upon what is affirmed, in respect of what is affirmed, or upon what is negated, in respect of what is negated. And it is either that the falsehood in it occurs for the attribution itself in that time for what is affirmed, or the affirmation in that time for what is negated. And as for affirming what is affirmed in other times and negating what is negated in other times, with variation in the quantity of the proposition if it is determinate.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage discusses propositions where the condition is a specific time. The condition specifies that the predicate holds for the subject only in that time, not in others. The author explains that the true contrary is formed by negating the proposition in a way that respects the time condition. The falsehood can occur either because the attribution itself is false in that time, or because the affirmation/negation is false. The author also notes that changing the time and adjusting the quantity (if the proposition is quantified) produces additional logical relations. This is a foundational concept for temporal logic in the Islamic tradition.

Key Terms

النقيض الحقيقي

Key Terms from Source Passage

The true contrary of a time-conditioned proposition must negate the predicate within the same time condition, not simply change the time.

True Contrary of General Possibility Propositions
Author defines true contrary and consequences for universal affirmative with general possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the true contrary of a universal affirmative modal proposition with general possibility and derive its logical consequence.

Core Idea
The contrary of a general possibility proposition negates the modality, not just the predicate.

In modal logic, understanding how contrary propositions work is essential for valid reasoning. This passage from Daqaiq al-Haqaiq explains how to find the true contrary of a universal affirmative with general possibility (imkan 'amm), and what follows from it. The method applies to all modal propositions and is crucial for sound argumentation in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modal proposition type
The passage specifies a universal affirmative proposition with general possibility, e.g., 'Every B is J with general possibility.' Recognize the modality (general possibility) and quantity (universal affirmative).
2
Recall the definition of general possibility
General possibility (al-imkan al-'amm) is the negation of impossibility; it includes both actual and possible. It does not exclude necessity here.
In later texts, it may be narrower, but in this passage it is broad.
3
Form the true contrary
The true contrary (al-naqid al-haqiqi) is formed by negating the modality along with the entire proposition: 'Not with general possibility: every B is J.' This contradicts the original proposition.
The contrary negates the modal qualifier, not just the predicate.
4
Derive the necessary consequence
From the contrary, deduce that 'Necessarily, some B is not J.' This follows because if the original is false (the contrary is true), then the affirmative fails under all modalities except impossibility, so necessity of the negative partial is required.
The consequence is a particular negative necessary proposition.
5
Apply the method to other propositions
The passage states that you must consider the true contrary and its consequent for the remaining proposition types (e.g., particular, negative). Practice with other modal combinations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان الإمكان بالمعنى العام فالقضية إما محصورة أو مخصوصة، فإن كانت محصورة فهي إما كليّة أو جزئية، فإن كانت كلية فإما موجبة أو سالبة، فإن كانت موجبة كقولنا : ((كل) "ب" فهو "ج" بالإمكان العام فنقيضها الحقيقي ليس بالإمكان العام كل "ب" فهو "ج") وما يلزمه فـ (بالضرورة بعض ما هو "ب" ليس هو "ج") إما أنه جزئي سالب فلما عرف من قبل، وإما أنه (بالضرورة) فلأنه إذا كذب الإيجاب بالإمكان العام فقد كذب بجهة الضرورة والإمكان الخاص، ويلزمه الامتناع بالضرورة، وعليك باعتبار النقيض الحقيقي ولازمه في ما بقي من القضايا.
And if the possibility is in the general sense, then the proposition is either determinate or indeterminate. If determinate, it is either universal or particular; if universal, either affirmative or negative. If affirmative, like our saying: 'Every B is J with general possibility,' its true contrary is 'Not with general possibility: every B is J,' and what follows from it is 'Necessarily some B is not J.' It is particular negative as known before, and it is necessary because when the affirmative with general possibility is false, it is also false with necessity and special possibility, and impossibility follows by necessity. And you must consider the true contrary and its consequent for the remaining propositions.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage covers modal propositions with possibility in the general sense (imkan bil-ma'na al-'amm). The author explains that for a universal affirmative modal proposition like 'Every B is J with general possibility,' its true contrary is 'Not with general possibility: every B is J.' The logical consequence of that contrary is 'Necessarily some B is not J.' The reasoning is that if the original is false (i.e., it is not true that every B is possibly J), then it must be necessary that some B is not J. This shows the relationship between possibility and necessity. The author reminds the reader that this method applies to all remaining propositions.

Key Terms
الإمكان العام
النقيض الحقيقي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Not with general possibility: every B is J
B) No B is J with general possibility
C) Some B is not J with general possibility
D) Every B is not J with general possibility

Key Takeaway
The true contrary of a universal affirmative with general possibility is the negation of the modality along with the same proposition, which yields a necessary particular negative.

Contrary and Consequences of Universal Affirmative with Special Possibility
Author defines true contrary and consequences for universal affirmative with special possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the true contrary of a universal affirmative proposition with special possibility and determine its necessary consequences.

Core Idea
Special possibility's contrary forces a disjunction of two necessities: either necessarily some B is J or necessarily some B is not J.

The author now turns to special possibility (al-imkan al-khass), which excludes necessity entirely. When a universal affirmative proposition is stated with special possibility, its true contrary is not simply the negation of the same proposition. Instead, the falsehood of special possibility leaves only necessity, but which necessity is undetermined. Understanding this relationship is crucial for modal logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition
Recognize that the proposition is universal affirmative with special possibility: 'Every B is J with special possibility.'
2
Determine its true contrary
The true contrary is not the contradictory (which would be some not J), but rather 'Not every B is J with special possibility.' This is the exact negation of the modal quality.
3
Derive the consequence
When the contrary is true, the original proposition is false. Since special possibility is false, necessity must hold for some part. There are two possibilities: either necessarily some B is J, or necessarily some B is not J. Both are necessary because the negation of special possibility (which is the negation of the negation of necessity) yields necessity.
4
Note the indeterminacy
The author stresses that one of these two necessities holds, but it is not known which. The consequent is a disjunction, not a single determinate claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان الإمكان بالمعنى الخاص فإن كانت القضية كليّة موجبة كقولنا : (كل) "ب" "ج" بالإمكان الخاص، فنقيضها الحقيقي ليس كل "ب" "ج" بالإمكان الخاص) ولازمه إما بالضرورة بعض "ب" "ج") وإما بالضرورة بعض "ب" ليس هو "ج" أما أخذ (البعض) فلنفسه، وأما أخذ (الضرورة سلباً وإيجاباً فلأنه مهما كذب الإمكان الخاص لم يبق غير ضرورة الإيجاب أو ضرورة السلب وأحدهما غير متعين واعتبر النقائض الحقيقية ولوازمها في ما بقي بنفسك.
And if the possibility is in the special sense, then if the proposition is universal affirmative, like our saying: 'Every B is J with special possibility,' its true contrary is 'Not every B is J with special possibility,' and its consequent is either 'Necessarily some B is J' or 'Necessarily some B is not J.' The taking of 'some' is for itself, and the taking of 'necessity' whether negative or affirmative is because whenever special possibility is false, nothing remains except necessity of affirmation or necessity of negation, and one of them is indeterminate. And consider the true contraries and their consequences for the remaining cases yourself.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage continues with possibility in the special sense (al-imkan al-khass). For a universal affirmative proposition like 'Every B is J with special possibility,' the true contrary is 'Not every B is J with special possibility.' The consequent of this contrary is that either necessarily some B is J or necessarily some B is not J. The reason is that if the special possibility (which excludes necessity) is false, then the proposition must be necessary in some part. The author notes that it is uncertain which of the two necessities holds. This logical point is important for understanding the relationship between possibility and necessity. The reader is encouraged to apply this analysis to the remaining cases.

Modal Oppositions: True Contrary of More Specific Possibility
Author defines true contrary for universal affirmative with more specific possibility (first subtype).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the true contrary of a universal affirmative proposition with the first subtype of more specific possibility and list the three possible consequents.

Core Idea
Negating a possibility proposition yields a triple disjunction of necessity.

Modal logic gets intricate when possibility and necessity interlock. The more specific possibility (al-imkan al-akhass) in its first subtype excludes both absolute and temporal necessity. Its true contrary does not simply negate the modality but branches into three distinct logical outcomes. This lesson unpacks that analysis as presented by the author.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of the first subtype
The first subtype of more specific possibility is defined as the negation of both absolute necessity (al-darura al-mutlaqa) and temporal necessity (al-darura al-waqtiyya). This means the proposition claims existence or non-existence without any necessity, whether unconditional or time-bound.
This subtype is contrasted with other meanings of al-imkan al-akhass.
2
Identify the universal affirmative with this modality
The author gives the example: 'Every B is J with possibility, i.e., without absolute or temporal necessity.' Recognize this as a universal affirmative proposition (kuliyya mujiba) modalized by this specific possibility.
The modality qualifies the copula, not the terms.
3
Determine the true contrary
The true contrary of 'Every B is J with this possibility' is 'Not every B is J with this possibility' – a particular negative. This is because the contradictory would be 'Some B is not J with this possibility' and the contrary is weaker.
The author shows that the falsehood in some part is certain.
4
Analyze the source of falsehood in some part
For some instance of B, the proposition is false. The falsehood can arise either from the categorical predicate 'it is J' (so the permanent negative 'always some B is not J' holds) or from the modal condition 'without necessity' (so the affirmative is true with absolute or temporal necessity for that instance).
Thus the negation splits the modal condition.
5
Deduce the three consequents
Therefore, the true contrary of the original proposition leads to a triple disjunction: (1) always some B is not J, (2) with absolute necessity some B is J, or (3) with temporal necessity some B is J.
This shows modal opposition is not simple one-to-one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان الإمكان بالمعنى الأخص فإما بمعنى ما انتفت عنه الضرورة المطلقة والوقتية، أو بمعنى ما هو مستقل الحال لا ضرورة في وجوده ولا عدمه لا ضرورة مطلقة ولا مشروطة لا بدّية كما سبق فإن كان الأول فنقيض الكلية الموجبة منه كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج" (بالإمكان أي من غير ضرورة مطلقة ولا وقتية ليس كل "ب" "ج" (بالإمكان المذكور، والكذب في البعض متيقن ثم ذلك البعض إما أن يكون الكذب فيه لقولنا: (هو "ج") فيصدق السلب الدائم، كان ضرورياً أو اتفاقياً، وأما لقولنا: (من غير ضرورة (مطلقة أو لقولنا ولا وقتية) فيصدق الإيجاب إما بالضرورة المطلقة، وإما بالضرورة الوقتية، فكان لذلك لازم النقيض (إما دائماً بعض "ب" ليس هو "ج")، وإما بالضرورة المطلقة بعض "ب" "ج") وإما بالضرورة الوقتية بعض "ب" "ج") وعليك باعتبار ذلك في ما بقي من القضايا.
But if the possibility is in the more specific sense, then it is either in the sense that absolute and temporal necessity are negated, or in the sense that the case is independent, with no necessity in its existence or non-existence, no absolute or conditional necessity, as before. If it is the first, then the true contrary of the universal affirmative from it, like our saying: 'Every B is J with possibility, i.e., without absolute or temporal necessity' is 'Not every B is J with the mentioned possibility.' And falsehood in some part is certain; then that part is either false due to our saying 'it is J' so the permanent negative is true, whether necessary or accidental; or due to our saying 'without absolute or temporal necessity' so the affirmative is true either with absolute necessity or with temporal necessity. Therefore, the consequent of the contrary is either 'Always some B is not J,' or 'With absolute necessity some B is J,' or 'With temporal necessity some B is J.' And you must consider that in the remaining propositions.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage addresses the more specific possibility (al-imkan al-akhass) in its first subtype, where absolute necessity and temporal necessity are negated. The author gives the example: 'Every B is J with possibility, i.e., without absolute or temporal necessity.' The true contrary negates this: 'Not every B is J with that possibility.' Since some part of the proposition is necessarily false, the cause of falsehood can be either the predicate 'is J' (resulting in a permanent negative) or the modal condition 'without necessity' (resulting in an affirmative with absolute or temporal necessity). The consequent of the contrary thus branches into three possibilities: a universal negative ('always some B is not J'), or a necessary affirmative (absolute or temporal). This detailed analysis shows the complexity of modal oppositions.

Key Terms
الإمكان الأخص
الضرورة المطلقة
الضرورة الوقتية

Second Subtype of More Specific Possibility: Future Contingency
Author begins describing second subtype of more specific possibility (future contingency).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the second subtype of more specific possibility (future contingency) and state its true contradictory.

Core Idea
Future contingency is possibility that depends on the future state, not necessity.

In the previous lesson, we learned about the first subtype of more specific possibility: possibility contrasted with necessity. Now the author describes the second subtype: future contingency. This type deals with what can happen in the future but is not necessary either absolutely or temporarily. By understanding this, you will be able to correctly form the negation (true contradictory) of such modal propositions, which is essential for analyzing arguments about future events in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modal qualifier
Look at the example: 'Every B is J with possibility in the future state (mustaqbal al-hal), without absolute or temporal necessity.' The modal qualifier 'with possibility in the future state' defines the second subtype.
The qualifier indicates that the modality is future contingency, not simple possibility.
2
Extract the definition
The second subtype of more specific possibility is future contingency: a proposition that is possibly true in the future but not necessarily true at any time. It is more specific than absolute possibility because it adds a temporal condition.
This contrasts with the first subtype which is possibility absolutely (opposed to necessity).
3
Find the true contradictory
The author states that the true contradictory (naqid) of the universal affirmative 'Every B is J with that possibility' is 'Not every B is J with that possibility.' This is the standard contradictory of a universal affirmative in modal logic.
The contradictory must be true exactly when the original is false.
4
Understand the falsehood condition
If the original proposition is false, the falsehood arises from the subject-predicate combination. Specifically, if the falsehood lies in the predicate 'is J', then the permanent negative (al-salb al-da'im) in the future state becomes true. This means that for all future times, B is not J.
This condition helps determine which specific contrary or contradictory holds based on the source of falsehood.

This passage introduces the second subtype of the more specific possibility, which is future contingency (imkan istiqbali). The example is: 'Every B is J in the future without absolute or temporal necessity.' The true contrary is the negation: 'Not every B is J with that possibility.' The falsehood of the original means that some part is false; if the falsehood lies in the predicate 'is J,' then the permanent negative holds for the future. The text is cut off, but the pattern is similar to the first subtype. This completes the discussion of modal possibilities.

Absolute Temporal Modality and Its Contradictory
Author explains contradictory of absolute temporal universal affirmative

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the true contradictory of a universal affirmative proposition with absolute temporal modality and list its necessary consequences for a conjunctive predicate.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a temporal universal affirmative negates the time and changes to particular negative.

In Islamic logic, modal propositions often involve time and necessity. This lesson walks you through the case of absolute temporal modality, where the predicate holds for all individuals at all times in a specified period. Understanding the precise contradictory is crucial for valid reasoning in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition
Find the subject, predicate (which may be a conjunction), and the temporal mode. In the example, the proposition is 'absolutely temporally every B is J D A'.
The predicate is a conjunction of three attributes: J, D, A.
2
Form the contradictory
Negate the mode ('not absolutely temporally'), change universal to particular, affirmative to negative. The contradictory is 'not absolutely temporally some B is J D A'.
This is the true contradictory (naqid haqiqi) according to the author.
3
Determine the necessary consequence
The necessary consequence (lazim) of the contradictory is a particular negative in all of the supposed time: 'some B is not J D A in all that time'.
Because falsehood may attach to any attribute, the consequence can take multiple forms.
4
List all combinations
The text enumerates seven possibilities: denying all three attributes, any two, or any one. For example, 'some B is not J D in all that time', 'some B is not J A in all that time', etc.
The pattern applies to any number of conjunctive attributes.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

"ج" "د" "أ" في بعض أوقات كون الموضوع متصفاً بـ "ب".
If it [the proposition] is with absolute temporal [modality] as in our saying: 'absolutely temporally every B is J D A', then its true contradictory is 'not absolutely temporally some B is J D A'. And it is known that its necessary consequence [from the contradictory], inasmuch as it is a universal affirmative absolute temporal, is a particular negative in all of the supposed time, and because falsehood might also attach to the attributes, the necessary consequence is either (some B is not J D A in all that time) or (some B is not J D in all that time) or (some B is not J A in all that time) or (some B is not D A in all that time) or (some B is not J in all that time) or (some B is not D in all that time) or (some B is not A in all that time). And it is upon you to consider the remaining divisions of propositions by yourself, and likewise for additional attributes. As for if the proposition is modal, then its mode is either necessity or possibility. If it is necessity, then either absolute necessity or conditional necessity. If it is absolute necessity, as in our saying: 'necessarily every B is J D A', then its true contradictory is 'not necessarily every B is J D A'. And it is known that the necessary consequence of its contradictory, inasmuch as it is a universal affirmative necessary, is (it is possible in the general sense that some B is not J), and since here it is possible to attribute falsehood to the attributes of the predicate or some of them, the necessary consequence is either (some B may possibly not, in the general sense, be J nor D nor A) or (some B may possibly not be J) or (some B may possibly not be D) or (some B may possibly not be A) or (some B may possibly not be J and D) or (some B may possibly not be D and A). And if the necessity is conditional, then either from the kind where the predicate in it is perpetual with the perpetuity of the condition and the condition is perpetual with the perpetuity of the subject, or from the other divisions. If it is the first, as in our saying: (Everything that is described by B perpetually with the perpetuity of the subject is J D A as long as it is B) then its true contradictory is 'Not everything that is described by B perpetually with the perpetuity of the subject is J D A as long as it is B', and it is known that the necessary consequence of the contradictory of this proposition when only the predicate is mentioned is a particular negative in some of the times when the subject is qualified by B, but since falsehood here may attach to all the attributes of the predicate or some of them, the necessary consequence is either (some of what is B perpetually with the perpetuity of the subject is not, in some of the times of its being B, J nor D nor A) or (not J) or (not D) or (not A) or (not J and not D) or (not J and not A) or (not D and not A). And if the condition is perpetual with the perpetuity of the subject but the predicate is not perpetual with its perpetuity, as in our saying: (Everything that is B perpetually with the perpetuity of the subject is J D A in some of the times when the subject is qualified by B), then its true contradictory is 'Not everything that is B perpetually with the perpetuity of the subject is J D A in some of the times when the subject is qualified by B', and it is known that its necessary consequence when the subject is stripped of the attributes is a particular perpetual both positively and negatively, and since here falsehood might attach to all the attributes of the predicate or some of them, the necessary consequence is either (some of what is perpetually B is J D A as long as it is B) or (not J nor D nor A as long as it is B) or (not J) or (not D) or (not A) or (not J D) or (not J A) or (not D A). And if the predicate is perpetual with the perpetuity of the condition but the condition is not perpetual with the perpetuity of the subject, as in our saying: (Everything that is described by B, not perpetually, is J D A as long as it is B), then its true contradictory is 'Not everything that is described by B, not perpetually, is J D A as long as it is B', and it is known that its necessary consequence when the predicate is stripped of the attributes is a particular perpetual positively or negatively, so the necessary consequence is either (some of what is B not perpetually with the perpetuity of the subject is not J D A at a time of its being B) or (not J) or (not D) or (not A) or (not J D) or (not J A) or (not D A). And if the predicate is affirmed of the subject in some of the times when the subject is qualified by the condition, and the condition is not perpetual for the subject, as in our saying: (Every B not perpetually is J D A in some of the times when the subject is qualified by B), then its true contradictory is (Some B not perpetually is not J D A in some of the times when the subject is qualified by B).
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is a technical exposition of modal categorical propositions and their logical contradictories. It belongs to the science of logic (mantiq) and focuses on the precise formulation of the true contradictory (al-naqid al-haqiqi) for various types of universal affirmative propositions qualified by time or modality. The author proceeds systematically, first handling the case of absolute temporal (al-itlaq al-waqti) propositions. He explains that if we have a universal affirmative with absolute temporal modality, e.g., 'Every B is J D A at all times in a given period,' then its contradictory is not simply 'Some B is not J D A at that period' but must account for the possibility that falsehood attaches to the combination of predicates. The necessary consequent (lazim) of the contradictory is a particular negative with the same time constraint, but because the predicate may be a conjunction of attributes (J, D, A), the contradictory can take multiple forms: it could deny all three attributes, or any two, or any one. The author leaves it to the reader to extend this to other propositional forms. Next, he turns to modal propositions (qadaya mujawwaha) with necessity (darura) or possibility (imkan). For absolute necessity, the contradictory of 'Necessarily every B is J D A' is 'Not necessarily every B is J D A,' which entails that it is possible (in the general sense) that some B is not J, and similarly for the attributes. He then treats conditional necessity (darura mashruta), dividing it into cases based on whether the predicate is perpetual with the condition and the condition perpetual with the subject. For each subcase, he states the contradictory and lists the possible necessary consequents when falsehood attaches to the predicate attributes (sifat al-mahmul). The author enumerates three conditional modalities: (1) both predicate and condition perpetual, (2) condition perpetual but predicate not, (3) predicate perpetual but condition not, and (4) neither perpetual (predicate affirmed only in some times). For each, he gives the contradictory and a set of possible negative consequents that cover all combinations of the three attributes (J, D, A). The passage is a formal logical exercise, using placeholder letters (ب for subject, ج، د، أ for predicate components) to illustrate the general pattern. The overall goal is to teach the reader how to determine the contradictory of any modal categorical proposition, which is essential for constructing valid arguments and avoiding fallacies.

Key Terms
نقيض حقيقي
الإطلاق الوقتي
لازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the true contradictory of 'Absolutely temporally every B is J D A'?
A) Absolutely temporally some B is not J
B) Not absolutely temporally some B is J D A
C) Not absolutely temporally every B is J D A
D) Absolutely temporally every B is not J D A

Key Takeaway
The contradictory of a temporal universal affirmative must negate the temporal mode and change the quantity and quality, and if the predicate is conjunctive, multiple forms are possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the proposition 'Absolutely temporally every rizq (provision) is halal, tayyib, mubarak.' Identify its true contradictory and list the seven possible forms of necessary consequence.

Propositions
intermediate
10 min
Absolute Necessity and Its Contradictory
Author explains contradictory of absolute necessity universal affirmative

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the true contradictory of a universal affirmative proposition with absolute necessity and list the possible modal consequences.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a necessary proposition is a possibility claim, not a negation of necessity.

When a proposition is asserted as absolutely necessary, its contradictory is not a simple negation; rather, it becomes a possibility. This lesson examines how the contradictory of 'Necessarily every B is J D A' leads to the possibility that some B lacks that attribute.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the modal type
The proposition has absolute necessity (darura mutlaqa): 'Necessarily every B is J D A'.
Necessity here means the predicate holds for every instance of the subject at all times without condition.
2
Form the contradictory
Negate the necessity mode: 'Not necessarily every B is J D A' (i.e., it is possible that some B is not J D A).
The contradictory is a possibility proposition, not a universal negative.
3
Extract the necessary consequence
The necessary consequence (lazim) of the contradictory is a particular possibility: 'some B may possibly not be J' (in the general sense of possibility).
The author uses 'imkan 'amm' (general possibility) which includes possible non-existence.
4
Account for conjunctive predicate
If falsehood may attach to all or some of the predicate attributes, the consequence can be any combination: e.g., 'some B may possibly not be J nor D nor A', or 'not J', etc.
Six forms are listed, covering all subsets of the three attributes.

If it [the proposition] is with absolute temporal [modality] as in our saying: 'absolutely temporally every B is J D A', then its true contradictory is 'not absolutely temporally some B is J D A'. And it is known that its necessary consequence [from the contradictory], inasmuch as it is a universal affirmative absolute temporal, is a particular negative in all of the supposed time, and because falsehood might also attach to the attributes, the necessary consequence is either (some B is not J D A in all that time) or (some B is not J D in all that time) or (some B is not J A in all that time) or (some B is not D A in all that time) or (some B is not J in all that time) or (some B is not D in all that time) or (some B is not A in all that time). As for if the proposition is modal, then its mode is either necessity or possibility. If it is necessity, then either absolute necessity or conditional necessity. If it is absolute necessity, as in our saying: 'necessarily every B is J D A', then its true contradictory is 'not necessarily every B is J D A'. And it is known that the necessary consequence of its contradictory, inasmuch as it is a universal affirmative necessary, is (it is possible in the general sense that some B is not J), and since here it is possible to attribute falsehood to the attributes of the predicate or some of them, the necessary consequence is either (some B may possibly not, in the general sense, be J nor D nor A) or (some B may possibly not be J) or (some B may possibly not be D) or (some B may possibly not be A) or (some B may possibly not be J and D) or (some B may possibly not be D and A). Commentary
This passage is a technical exposition of modal categorical propositions and their logical contradictories. It belongs to the science of logic (mantiq) and focuses on the precise formulation of the true contradictory (al-naqid al-haqiqi) for various types of universal affirmative propositions qualified by time or modality. The author proceeds systematically, first handling the case of absolute temporal (al-itlaq al-waqti) propositions. He explains that if we have a universal affirmative with absolute temporal modality, e.g., 'Every B is J D A at all times in a given period,' then its contradictory is not simply 'Some B is not J D A at that period' but must account for the possibility that falsehood attaches to the combination of predicates. The necessary consequent (lazim) of the contradictory is a particular negative with the same time constraint, but because the predicate may be a conjunction of attributes (J, D, A), the contradictory can take multiple forms: it could deny all three attributes, or any two, or any one. The author leaves it to the reader to extend this to other propositional forms. Next, he turns to modal propositions (qadaya mujawwaha) with necessity (darura) or possibility (imkan). For absolute necessity, the contradictory of 'Necessarily every B is J D A' is 'Not necessarily every B is J D A,' which entails that it is possible (in the general sense) that some B is not J, and similarly for the attributes. He then treats conditional necessity (darura mashruta), dividing it into cases based on whether the predicate is perpetual with the condition and the condition perpetual with the subject. For each subcase, he states the contradictory and lists the possible necessary consequents when falsehood attaches to the predicate attributes (sifat al-mahmul). The author enumerates three conditional modalities: (1) both predicate and condition perpetual, (2) condition perpetual but predicate not, (3) predicate perpetual but condition not, and (4) neither perpetual (predicate affirmed only in some times). For each, he gives the contradictory and a set of possible negative consequents that cover all combinations of the three attributes (J, D, A). The passage is a formal logical exercise, using placeholder letters (ب for subject, ج، د، أ for predicate components) to illustrate the general pattern. The overall goal is to teach the reader how to determine the contradictory of any modal categorical proposition, which is essential for constructing valid arguments and avoiding fallacies.

Key Takeaway

Propositions
intermediate
10 min
Conditional Necessity (Perpetual Predicate and Condition)
Author explains contradictory of conditional necessity universal affirmative (first subcase)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the true contradictory of a universal affirmative proposition with conditional necessity where the predicate and condition are perpetual.

Core Idea
Conditional necessity ties the predicate to the duration of the subject, and the contradictory must negate that tie.

In conditional necessity, the predicate holds as long as the subject satisfies a condition. The author distinguishes cases based on the perpetuity of the predicate and condition. This lesson examines the case where both the predicate and the condition are perpetual with the subject.

Guided Path
1
Understand the conditional modality
The proposition: 'Everything that is B perpetually (with the perpetuity of the subject) is J D A as long as it is B.' That is, as long as something is B, it has attributes J, D, A.
Perpetuity here means the subject is B at all times (daim bi dawam al-mawdu').
2
Form the contradictory
The contradictory is 'Not everything that is B perpetually is J D A as long as it is B.' This negates the universal quantifier while keeping the condition.
The contradictory is not 'everything is not' but 'not everything is'.
3
Determine the necessary consequence
The necessary consequence (lazim) is a particular negative in some of the times when the subject is B: 'some of what is B perpetually is not J D A in some of the times of its being B'.
The consequence is restricted to some times, not all times.
4
List possibilities for conjunctive predicate
Falsehood may attach to all or some attributes, so the consequence can be any of seven forms: denying all three, any two, or any one of J, D, A.
This mirrors the pattern from earlier modalities.

دائماً بدوام الموضوع فهو "ج" "د" "أ" ما دام "ب"، وقد عرف أن لازم نقيض هذه القضية عند ذكر المحمول فقط جزئي سالب في بعض أوقات كون الموضوع متصفاً بـ "ب" لكنه لما كان الكذب هاهنا قد يعود إلى صفات المحمول (ليس "ج" "د") وإما (ليس "ج" "أ") وإما (ليس "د" "أ"). وأما إن كان المحمول دائماً بدوام الشرط لكن الشرط غير دائم بدوام الموضوع كقولنا: (كل ما هو موصوف بـ "ب" لا دائماً فهو "ج" "د" "أ" ما دام "ب")، فنقيضها الحقيقي ليس كل ما هو موصوف بـ "ب" لا دائماً فهو "ج" "د" "أ" ما دام "ب"، وقد عرف أن لازمه عندما إذا كان المحمول مجرداً عن الصفات أنه جزئي دائم إيجاباً أو سلباً، فاللازم إما بعض ما هو "ب" لا دائماً بدوام الموضوع ليس هو "ج" "د" "أ" في وقت من أوقات كونه "ب") وإما (ليس هو "ج") وإما (ليس هو "د") وإما (ليس هو "أ") وإما (ليس "ج" "د") وإما (ليس "ج" "أ") وإما (ليس "د" "أ"). وإن كان المحمول ثابتاً للموضوع في بعض أوقات كون الموضوع متصفاً بالشرط، والشرط غير دائم للموضوع كقولنا: (كل "ب" لا دائماً فهو "ج" "د" "أ" في بعض أوقات كون الموضوع متصفاً بـ "ب") فنقيضها الحقيقي بعض ما هو "ب" لا دائماً ليس هو "ج" "د" "أ" في بعض أوقات كون الموضوع متصفاً بـ "ب".
If it [the proposition] is with absolute temporal [modality] as in our saying: 'absolutely temporally every B is J D A', then its true contradictory is 'not absolutely temporally some B is J D A'. And it is known that its necessary consequence [from the contradictory], inasmuch as it is a universal affirmative absolute temporal, is a particular negative in all of the supposed time, and because falsehood might also attach to the attributes, the necessary consequence is either (some B is not J D A in all that time) or (some B is not J D in all that time) or (some B is not J A in all that time) or (some B is not D A in all that time) or (some B is not J in all that time) or (some B is not D in all that time) or (some B is not A in all that time). And it is upon you to consider the remaining divisions of propositions by yourself, and likewise for additional attributes. As for if the proposition is modal, then its mode is either necessity or possibility. If it is necessity, then either absolute necessity or conditional necessity. If it is absolute necessity, as in our saying: 'necessarily every B is J D A', then its true contradictory is 'not necessarily every B is J D A'. And it is known that the necessary consequence of its contradictory, inasmuch as it is a universal affirmative necessary, is (it is possible in the general sense that some B is not J), and since here it is possible to attribute falsehood to the attributes of the predicate or some of them, the necessary consequence is either (some B may possibly not, in the general sense, be J nor D nor A) or (some B may possibly not be J) or (some B may possibly not be D) or (some B may possibly not be A) or (some B may possibly not be J and D) or (some B may possibly not be D and A). And if the necessity is conditional, then either from the kind where the predicate in it is perpetual with the perpetuity of the condition and the condition is perpetual with the perpetuity of the subject, or from the other divisions. If it is the first, as in our saying: (Everything that is described by B perpetually with the perpetuity of the subject is J D A as long as it is B) then its true contradictory is 'Not everything that is described by B perpetually with the perpetuity of the subject is J D A as long as it is B', and it is known that the necessary consequence of the contradictory of this proposition when only the predicate is mentioned is a particular negative in some of the times when the subject is qualified by B, but since falsehood here may attach to all the attributes of the predicate or some of them, the necessary consequence is either (some of what is B perpetually with the perpetuity of the subject is not, in some of the times of its being B, J nor D nor A) or (not J) or (not D) or (not A) or (not J and not D) or (not J and not A) or (not D and not A). And if the condition is perpetual with the perpetuity of the subject but the predicate is not perpetual with its perpetuity, as in our saying: (Everything that is B perpetually with the perpetuity of the subject is J D A in some of the times when the subject is qualified by B), then its true contradictory is 'Not everything that is B perpetually with the perpetuity of the subject is J D A in some of the times when the subject is qualified by B', and it is known that its necessary consequence when the subject is stripped of the attributes is a particular perpetual both positively and negatively, and since here falsehood might attach to all the attributes of the predicate or some of them, the necessary consequence is either (some of what is perpetually B is J D A as long as it is B) or (not J nor D nor A as long as it is B) or (not J) or (not D) or (not A) or (not J D) or (not J A) or (not D A). And if the predicate is perpetual with the perpetuity of the condition but the condition is not perpetual with the perpetuity of the subject, as in our saying: (Everything that is described by B, not perpetually, is J D A as long as it is B), then its true contradictory is 'Not everything that is described by B, not perpetually, is J D A as long as it is B', and it is known that its necessary consequence when the predicate is stripped of the attributes is a particular perpetual positively or negatively, so the necessary consequence is either (some of what is B not perpetually with the perpetuity of the subject is not J D A at a time of its being B) or (not J) or (not D) or (not A) or (not J D) or (not J A) or (not D A). And if the predicate is affirmed of the subject in some of the times when the subject is qualified by the condition, and the condition is not perpetual for the subject, as in our saying: (Every B not perpetually is J D A in some of the times when the subject is qualified by B), then its true contradictory is (Some B not perpetually is not J D A in some of the times when the subject is qualified by B).
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is a technical exposition of modal categorical propositions and their logical contradictories. It belongs to the science of logic (mantiq) and focuses on the precise formulation of the true contradictory (al-naqid al-haqiqi) for various types of universal affirmative propositions qualified by time or modality. The author proceeds systematically, first handling the case of absolute temporal (al-itlaq al-waqti) propositions. He explains that if we have a universal affirmative with absolute temporal modality, e.g., 'Every B is J D A at all times in a given period,' then its contradictory is not simply 'Some B is not J D A at that period' but must account for the possibility that falsehood attaches to the combination of predicates. The necessary consequent (lazim) of the contradictory is a particular negative with the same time constraint, but because the predicate may be a conjunction of attributes (J, D, A), the contradictory can take multiple forms: it could deny all three attributes, or any two, or any one. The author leaves it to the reader to extend this to other propositional forms. Next, he turns to modal propositions (qadaya mujawwaha) with necessity (darura) or possibility (imkan). For absolute necessity, the contradictory of 'Necessarily every B is J D A' is 'Not necessarily every B is J D A,' which entails that it is possible (in the general sense) that some B is not J, and similarly for the attributes. He then treats conditional necessity (darura mashruta), dividing it into cases based on whether the predicate is perpetual with the condition and the condition perpetual with the subject. For each subcase, he states the contradictory and lists the possible necessary consequents when falsehood attaches to the predicate attributes (sifat al-mahmul). The author enumerates three conditional modalities: (1) both predicate and condition perpetual, (2) condition perpetual but predicate not, (3) predicate perpetual but condition not, and (4) neither perpetual (predicate affirmed only in some times). For each, he gives the contradictory and a set of possible negative consequents that cover all combinations of the three attributes (J, D, A). The passage is a formal logical exercise, using placeholder letters (ب for subject, ج، د، أ for predicate components) to illustrate the general pattern. The overall goal is to teach the reader how to determine the contradictory of any modal categorical proposition, which is essential for constructing valid arguments and avoiding fallacies.

الضرورة المشروطة
لازم

A) Some B perpetually is not J D A as long as it is B
B) Not every B perpetually is J D A as long as it is B
C) Every B perpetually is not J D A as long as it is B
D) Some B perpetually is J D A as long as it is B

Key Takeaway
The contradictory of a conditional necessity proposition denies the universal claim while preserving the conditional structure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

True Contrary of Constant Condition Propositions
Defines true contrary and enumerates necessary consequences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the true contrary of a proposition with constant condition and non-constant predicate, and enumerate the possible necessary consequences.

Core Idea
The true contrary respects the condition and temporal qualifier, not just the basic contradictory.

This passage delves into a refined concept of the true contrary (al-naqīḍ al-ḥaqīqī) for propositions that combine a constant condition with a predicate that holds only at some times. You will learn how to construct the true contrary correctly and understand the multiple possibilities for the necessary consequence (al-lāzim) when the contrary is affirmed. This is a precise logical tool essential for advanced jadal and burhān.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
The passage describes a proposition: 'Every B, constantly as long as it is B, is J D A at some times when B.' Note that the condition 'as long as it is B' is constant, but the predicate (J, D, A) is not constant—it holds only at some times.
This is not a simple universal affirmative; it has temporal and conditional complexity.
2
Construct the true contrary
The true contrary is formed by negating the entire proposition while preserving the condition and temporal qualifier: 'Not every B, constantly as long as it is B, is J D A at some times when B.' This is different from a simple contradictory like 'Some B is not J D A at some times.'
The negation must scope over the whole quantified conditional.
3
Understand the necessary consequence
When the subject is stripped of its attributes (considered in itself), the necessary consequence of the true contrary is a particular (juz'ī) and constant (dā'im) proposition. This consequence can be positive or negative.
The consequence is not the original proposition but something that follows from its negation under abstraction.
4
Enumerate the possibilities
Since the falsehood of the original could be due to any combination of the predicate attributes (J, D, A) failing for some individuals, the necessary consequence must cover all possibilities. The passage lists 8 cases: e.g., 'Some B is J D A as long as it is B' (the original partially true), or 'Not J and not D and not A as long as it is B' (all false), plus combinations like 'J or not' (meaning some B is either J or not-J, etc.).
This enumeration reflects disjunctive possibilities, not a single determinate consequence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان الشرط دائماً بدوام الموضوع لكن المحمول غير دائم بدوامه كما في قولنا: كل ما هو "ب" دائماً بدوام الموضوع فهو "ج" "د" "أ" في بعض أوقات كون الموضوع متصفاً بـ "ب"، فنقيضها الحقيقي ليس كل ما هو "ب" دائماً بدوام الموضوع فهو "ج" "د" "أ" في بعض أوقات كون الموضوع متصفاً بـ "ب"، وقد عرف أن لازمه عند كون الموضوع مجرداً عن الصفات جزئي دائم إيجاباً وسلباً، وحيث احتمل عود الكذب هاهنا إلى كل صفات المحمول أو بعضها كان اللازم إما بعض ما هو دائماً "ب" فهو "ج" "د" "أ" ما دام "ب" وإما (ليس "ج" ولا "د" ولا "أ" ما دام "ب") وإما ("ج" أو ليس) وإما ("د" أو ليس) وإما ("أ" أو ليس) وإما ("ج" "د" "أ" أو ليس) وإما ("ج" "أ" أو ليس) وإما ("د" "أ" أو ليس).
And if the condition is constant with the constancy of the subject, but the predicate is not constant with its constancy, as in our saying: 'Everything that is 'B' constantly with the constancy of the subject is 'J' 'D' 'A' at some times when the subject is qualified by 'B',' then its true contrary is 'Not everything that is 'B' constantly with the constancy of the subject is 'J' 'D' 'A' at some times when the subject is qualified by 'B'.' And it is known that its necessary consequence when the subject is stripped of attributes is a particular constant in affirmation and negation. And since the possibility of the falsehood occurring here extends to all or some of the predicate's attributes, the necessary consequence is either: 'Some of what is constantly 'B' is 'J' 'D' 'A' as long as it is 'B',' or ('Not 'J' and not 'D' and not 'A' as long as it is 'B'), or ('J' or not), or ('D' or not), or ('A' or not), or ('J' 'D' 'A' or not), or ('J' 'A' or not), or ('D' 'A' or not).
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage defines the true contrary (al-naqīḍ al-ḥaqīqī) of a specific type of conditional proposition and then enumerates the possible necessary consequences (al-lāzim) when that contrary is true. The author is engaged in a detailed analysis of propositions that involve both a condition (sharṭ) and a predicate with multiple attributes (here represented by the letters J, D, A). The condition is constant with the constancy of the subject (e.g., 'as long as it is B'), but the predicate is not constant—meaning the predicate (J, D, A) holds only at some times when the subject is qualified by B. The true contrary is not a simple negation but must correctly reflect the condition and the temporal qualifier. The author then notes that when the subject is considered stripped of its attributes, the necessary consequence is a particular (juz'ī) and constant (dā'im) proposition in either affirmation or negation. Since the falsehood introduced by the true contrary could affect all or only some of the predicate's attributes, several possible necessary consequences arise, each covering different combinations of the attributes. This method is typical of advanced mantiq works that analyze the fine structure of propositions, especially in the context of the 'true contrary' (al-naqīḍ al-ḥaqīqī) used in jadal and burhān. For instance, in a fiqh context, if one says 'Every intoxicant (khamr) is haram under all conditions (sharṭ dā'im),' the true contrary would be 'Not every intoxicant is haram under all conditions,' which could imply that some intoxicant is not haram at some time, etc.
