Types of False Conditional
Classification of false conditional propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a false conditional proposition into three cases based on the truth values of its parts.

Core Idea
A conditional is false when the implication fails, not simply when its parts are false.

Conditional propositions are not automatically true or false based on the truth of their parts. The author of Daqa'iq al-Haqa'iq shows exactly how truth values of antecedent and consequent determine falsity. Let's break down the three cases he identifies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first case (both parts true)
The author says the first case occurs only in negative conditionals (السوالب). Why? Because if both parts are true, the affirmative conditional would be true. So the negative of that true conditional is false. Example: 'It is not the case that if man is an animal then he is a body' is false because the affirmative is true.
The implication is missing or accidental (اتفاقي) in this case.
2
Identify the second case (both parts false)
When the antecedent and consequent are both false, the conditional is false if there is no real implication. Example: 'If man is a horse, then he is a donkey' — both false and the implication does not hold.
The falsity of both parts does not itself make the conditional true; the implication must also be valid.
3
Identify the third case (one part true, one false)
When the antecedent is true and the consequent is false, the conditional is false. Example: 'If man is an animal, then he is a stone' — antecedent true, consequent false, implication fails.
This is the classic case of 'true antecedent, false consequent' rendering the conditional false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القضية الكاذبة فإما أن يكون جزاها صادقين أو كاذبين أو أحدهما صادق والآخر كاذب، فإن كان الأول فلا يكون في غير السوالب، إذ الجزآن مهما كانا صادقين كانت النسبة صادقة، وسواء كان لزوم التالي بوضع المقدم، أو أنه اتفاقي وذلك كما في قولنا: ليس إن كان الإنسان حيواناً فهو جسم، وإن كان الثاني فكما في قولنا: إن كان الإنسان فرساً فهو حمار، وإن كان الثالث فكما في قولنا: إن كان الإنسان حيواناً فهو حجر. وأما اللزوم الناقص فهو يكون التالي مما لا ينعكس على المقدم في اللزوم، وذلك عندما إذا كان التالي أعم من المقدم كما في قولنا: إن كان هذا إنساناً فهو حيوان فإنه يصدق ولا يصدق أنه إن كان حيواناً فهو إنسان إذ الأخص يلزمه الأعم، والأعم فلا يلزمه الأخص.
As for the false conditional, its parts are either both true, both false, or one true and the other false. If the first case, it cannot occur except in negatives, because whenever both parts are true the relation is true, whether the implication of the consequent by positing the antecedent is necessary or accidental. An example is our saying: 'It is not the case that if man is an animal then he is a body.' If the second case, it is like our saying: 'If man is a horse, then he is a donkey.' If the third case, like our saying: 'If man is an animal, then he is a stone.' As for incomplete implication, it is when the consequent is such that it does not imply the antecedent in the implication; that is when the consequent is more general than the antecedent, as in our saying: 'If this is a man, then it is an animal.' It is true, but it is not true that 'if it is an animal, then it is a man' because the specific implies the general, but the general does not imply the specific.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage classifies false conditional propositions and introduces the concept of incomplete implication (al-luzum al-naqis). A false conditional occurs when the implication is invalid. The author lists three possibilities for the truth values of the parts: (1) both parts true, (2) both false, (3) one true one false. The first type (both true) can only be false in negative conditionals (i.e., a negated conditional) because if both parts are true, the affirmative conditional would be true. Example: 'It is not the case that if man is an animal, then he is a body' is false because the affirmative is true. The second type (both false) is exemplified by 'If man is a horse, then he is a donkey'—both parts false, but the conditional is false because being a horse does not imply being a donkey. The third type (true antecedent, false consequent) is exemplified by 'If man is an animal, then he is a stone'—the antecedent is true, the consequent false, so the conditional is false. The author then defines incomplete implication: it occurs when the implication is not reversible, i.e., the consequent does not imply the antecedent. This happens when the consequent is more general than the antecedent, as in 'If this is a man, then it is an animal' (true), but the converse is false because animal does not imply man. The specific implies the general, but not vice versa.

Key Terms
القضية الكاذبة
السوالب

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) إن كان الإنسان فرساً فهو حمار

Key Takeaway
A false conditional can have both parts true (only in negated form), both false, or one true and one false; the implication must also be invalid.

Incomplete Implication (al-Luzum al-Naqis)
Definition of incomplete implication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify incomplete implication and explain why it is not reversible.

Core Idea
When the consequent is more general than the antecedent, the implication is one-way only.

Not every true conditional can be reversed. The author introduces the concept of incomplete implication (اللزوم الناقص) to describe conditionals where the consequent is broader in scope, making the converse false. This distinction is crucial for evaluating arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Understand incomplete implication
Incomplete implication occurs when the antecedent implies the consequent, but the consequent does not imply the antecedent. This is because the consequent is more general (أعم) than the antecedent.
Example: 'If this is a man, then it is an animal' is true, but 'If it is an animal, then it is a man' is false.
2
Test the reversibility
Take the example given: 'If this is a man, then it is an animal.' Check the converse: 'If it is an animal, then it is a man.' Since 'animal' includes many species, the converse is false. This shows incomplete implication.
The specific (أخص) implies the general (أعم), but not vice versa.
3
Apply to Islamic sciences
In usul al-fiqh, when a ruling is derived from an 'illah (cause), the implication from cause to ruling is often complete (both ways). But sometimes the 'illah is more specific than the ruling, leading to incomplete implication. For example, 'If something is khamr (wine), then it is forbidden' is true, but 'If it is forbidden, then it is khamr' is false because many things are forbidden besides wine.
This affects qiyas: the presence of the 'illah must be established for both directions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القضية الكاذبة فإما أن يكون جزاها صادقين أو كاذبين أو أحدهما صادق والآخر كاذب، فإن كان الأول فلا يكون في غير السوالب، إذ الجزآن مهما كانا صادقين كانت النسبة صادقة، وسواء كان لزوم التالي بوضع المقدم، أو أنه اتفاقي وذلك كما في قولنا: ليس إن كان الإنسان حيواناً فهو جسم، وإن كان الثاني فكما في قولنا: إن كان الإنسان فرساً فهو حمار، وإن كان الثالث فكما في قولنا: إن كان الإنسان حيواناً فهو حجر. وأما اللزوم الناقص فهو يكون التالي مما لا ينعكس على المقدم في اللزوم، وذلك عندما إذا كان التالي أعم من المقدم كما في قولنا: إن كان هذا إنساناً فهو حيوان فإنه يصدق ولا يصدق أنه إن كان حيواناً فهو إنسان إذ الأخص يلزمه الأعم، والأعم فلا يلزمه الأخص.
As for the false conditional, its parts are either both true, both false, or one true and the other false. Defining the Disjunctive Proposition
The author defines disjunctive propositions and distinguishes them from conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a disjunctive proposition and distinguish it from a conditional proposition based on the nature of the relation (infisal vs ittisal).

Core Idea
A disjunctive proposition affirms or denies opposition (inad) between two propositions, not a single categorical statement.

The author begins by defining the disjunctive proposition as that which affirms or denies opposition between two propositions. He warns against confusing it with a categorical proposition that merely states opposition. He also compares it with the conditional proposition, highlighting that the disjunctive's affirmative relation is by disconnection (infisal) while the conditional's is by connection (ittisal). However, both share the same predicative structure when analyzed. Let's break down these distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term 'al-qadiya al-munfasila' and note its definition: it affirms or denies opposition of one proposition to another.
2
Spot the contrast example
The author gives an example: 'The even is opposite to the odd' – this is a categorical proposition, not a disjunctive, even though it mentions opposition.
The key is that the proposition must present a choice between two complete propositions.
3
Compare with conditional
Note the difference: disjunctive has infisal (disconnection), conditional has ittisal (connection).
Affirmative relation is by disconnection for disjunctives, by connection for conditionals.
4
Recognize the shared structure
Both disjunctives and conditionals share a predicative relation for each part when analyzed.
This means each component part has its own subject-predicate structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القضية المنفصلة فهي ما توجب أو تسلب عناد قضية لأخرى، لا كما في قولنا: الزوج معاند للفرد ومنفصل عنه أو ليس معانداً فإن النسبة بينهما وإن كانت بالعناد فليست القضية إلا حَمْلية، وهي وإن فارقت المتصلة في أن النسبة الإيجابية لها بالانفصال، وللمتصلة بالاتصال، فمشاركة لها في النسبة الخبرية لكل واحد من جزأيها عند التحليل، وإنها منقسمة على حسب انقسام المتصلة إلى حقيقي وغير حقيقي، فأما الحقيقي من المنفصلة فما لفظه (إما) وإما فيها دالة على المنع من الجمع بين الأجزاء، والخلو منها، وهي إما أن تكون ذات جزأين فقط أو أكثر، فإن كان الأول فإما أن يكون أحد الجزأين نقيضاً للآخر كقولنا: العدد إما أن يكون
As for the disjunctive proposition, it is what affirms or denies opposition of one proposition to another, not as in our saying: 'The even is opposite to the odd and separated from it, or it is not opposite'—for the relation between them, even if it is opposition, the proposition is only categorical. And although it differs from the conditional in that its affirmative relation is by disconnection, and for the conditional by connection, it shares with it the predicative relation for each of its parts upon analysis. It is divided, according to the division of the conditional, into real and non-real. As for the real disjunctive, its wording is 'imma' (either), and 'imma' in it indicates prevention of conjunction between the parts and prevention of emptiness from them. It is either with only two parts or more. If the first, then either one of the parts is the contradictory of the other, like our saying: 'The number is either'—
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage introduces disjunctive propositions (al-qadiya al-munfasila). The author defines it as a proposition that affirms or denies opposition between two propositions. An example of a non-disjunctive use of opposition is 'The even is opposite to the odd' because that is a categorical proposition, not a disjunctive. Although the disjunctive differs from the conditional in that its affirmative relation is by disconnection (infisal) rather than connection (ittisal), it shares with the conditional the predicative relation each part has when analyzed. Disjunctives are divided into real (haqiqi) and non-real (ghayr haqiqi), following the division of conditionals. The real disjunctive is expressed by the particle 'imma' (either), which indicates prevention of both conjunction (jam') and emptiness (khulu') of the parts. A real disjunctive can have two or more parts. If it has two parts, one may be the contradictory (naqid) of the other. The example given begins: 'The number is either...' (cut off). This passage sets the foundation for analyzing disjunctive propositions, which are essential in logical reasoning, especially in establishing exclusive alternatives.

العناد

A disjunctive proposition is defined by affirming or denying opposition between propositions, and its distinctive feature is the disconnection (infisal) between its parts, unlike the connection in conditionals.

Real Disjunctive and the Condition of Exclusivity
The author defines real disjunctive and its conditions using 'imma'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a real disjunctive proposition by the particle 'imma' and verify that it fulfills the condition of preventing both conjunction and emptiness (man' al-jam' wa al-khulu').

Core Idea
The real disjunctive uses 'imma' to indicate that exactly one of the alternatives is true.

After defining disjunctive propositions in general, the author turns to the real disjunctive (al-haqiqiyya). He explains that the real disjunctive is expressed by the particle 'imma' (either), which signals that the parts are mutually exclusive (prevention of conjunction) and collectively exhaustive (prevention of emptiness). This ensures that one and only one part is true. He also notes that real disjunctives can have two or more parts, and if two, one part may be the contradictory of the other. Let's explore these features.

Guided Path
1
Identify the real disjunctive marker
Note that the author says the real disjunctive's wording is 'imma'.
2
Understand the condition
'Imma' indicates prevention of both jam' (both true together) and khulu' (both false together). That means exactly one part is true.
This is the essential logical property of a real disjunctive.
3
Distinguish by number of parts
Real disjunctives can have two or more parts. The passage begins discussing the two-part case.
4
Notice the contradictory relation
In a two-part real disjunctive, one part may be the contradictory (naqid) of the other, which automatically ensures exclusivity and exhaustiveness.
Example: 'The number is either even or odd' — even and odd are contradictories.

It is divided, according to the division of the conditional, into real and non-real. As for the real disjunctive, its wording is 'imma' (either), and 'imma' in it indicates prevention of conjunction between the parts and prevention of emptiness from them. Truth Conditions of Conditional Propositions and Incomplete Necessitation
Author explains true and false conditional propositions and defines incomplete implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a conditional proposition is true or false based on the author's criteria, and identify cases of incomplete necessitation.

Core Idea
A conditional is true only when the consequent necessarily follows; incomplete necessitation occurs when the consequent is more general.

In this passage, the author lays out the precise conditions under which a conditional proposition (qadiyya sharṭiyya) is considered true or false. He also introduces the important concept of incomplete necessitation (al-luzūm al-nāqiṣ), where the implication holds in one direction but not the reverse. Understanding these distinctions is essential for evaluating arguments that use conditional statements, especially in legal and theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parts of a conditional
For any given conditional, find the antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tālī). The antecedent is the clause after 'if', the consequent after 'then'.
2
Check the truth values of the parts
Determine whether the antecedent and consequent are individually true or false based on reality. The author notes three possibilities: both true, both false, or one true and one false.
3
Apply the author's truth condition
A conditional is true only if the consequent necessarily follows from the antecedent (essential or accidental necessitation). A conditional is false if the antecedent does not guarantee the consequent. For example, 'If man is a horse then he is a donkey' is false because the antecedent does not necessitate the consequent.
The author warns that a conditional with both parts false is false, contrary to modern material implication.
4
Test for incomplete necessitation
If the conditional is true, check whether the converse holds. If the consequent is more general than the antecedent, then the implication is incomplete: the antecedent implies the consequent, but not vice versa. For example, 'If this is a man then it is an animal' is true, but the converse is false because 'animal' is more general.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدم ولا انتفائه فهو حق لكن لا مطلقاً، بل فيها سواء المنحرفات من الشرطيات، والثاني فكما في قولنا: إن كانت الخمسة زوجاً فهي عدد فإنها وإن كان لزوم العدد للزوج بيناً، والخمسة عدداً فليست الخمسة زوجاً، وفي معنى هذا ما كان التالي فيه أعم من شيئين متقابلين، وجعل أحد المتقابلين مقدماً له والصادق في تلك القضية ليس إلا ما قابله وقد بينا عن أمثال هذا مغاليط في الأقيسة الكائنة عنها، سنبينه عليها في موضعها. وأما القضية الكاذبة فإما أن يكون جزاها صادقين أو كاذبين أو أحدهما صادق والآخر كاذب، فإن كان الأول فلا يكون في غير السوالب، إذ الجزآن مهما كانا صادقين كانت النسبة صادقة، وسواء كان لزوم التالي بوضع المقدم، أو أنه اتفاقي وذلك كما في قولنا: ليس إن كان الإنسان حيواناً فهو جسم وإن كان الثاني فكما في قولنا: إن كان الإنسان فرساً فهو حمار وإن كان الثالث فكما في قولنا: إن كان الإنسان حيواناً فهو حجر. وأما اللزوم الناقص فهو يكون التالي مما لا ينعكس على المقدم في اللزوم، وذلك عندما إذا كان التالي أعم من المقدم كما في قولنا: إن كان هذا إنساناً فهو حيوان فإنه يصدق ولا يصدق أنه إن كان حيواناً فهو إنسان إذ الأخص يلزمه الأعم، والأعم فلا يلزمه الأخص.
The antecedent and its negation is true but not absolutely, rather in it are the deviants of the conditionals. And the second is as in our saying: 'If five is even then it is a number', for even though the necessitation of number for even is evident, and five is a number, yet five is not even. And similar to this is what the consequent is more general than two contraries, and one of the contraries is made its antecedent, and the true in that proposition is only its opposite. And we have already shown about such things as fallacies in the syllogisms arising from them; we will explain them in their place. As for the false proposition, either its two parts are both true, or both false, or one true and the other false. If the first, then it does not occur except in negatives, since whenever the two parts are true the relation is true, whether the necessitation of the consequent by the antecedent is essential or accidental. That is as in our saying: 'It is not that if man is an animal then he is a body.' If the second, as in our saying: 'If man is a horse then he is a donkey.' If the third, as in our saying: 'If man is an animal then he is a stone.' As for incomplete necessitation, it is where the consequent does not reverse upon the antecedent in necessitation; this occurs when the consequent is more general than the antecedent, as in our saying: 'If this is a man then it is an animal,' for it is true, but it is not true that if it is an animal then it is a man, since the more specific necessitates the more general, but the more general does not necessitate the more specific.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage defines the truth conditions of conditional propositions (al-qadāyā al-sharṭiyya). The author explains that a conditional is true when the consequent follows from the antecedent, but not merely coincidentally. He distinguishes between true conditionals, where the consequent necessarily follows (even if the antecedent is false), and false conditionals, which occur in three forms: both parts true (only in negative conditionals like 'It is not that if man is an animal then he is a body'), both parts false (e.g., 'If man is a horse then he is a donkey'), or one true and one false (e.g., 'If man is an animal then he is a stone'). The key point is that a conditional is false when the truth of the antecedent does not guarantee the truth of the consequent. Then he discusses 'incomplete necessitation' (al-luzūm al-nāqiṣ), where the consequent is more general than the antecedent. For example, 'If this is a man then it is an animal' is true because man implies animal, but the converse is false because animal does not necessarily imply man. This introduces the concept of logical asymmetry between the specific and the general: the specific implies the general, but not vice versa. The passage also warns that such incomplete implications can lead to fallacious syllogisms, which the author promises to address later.

Key Terms
المقدم (al-muqaddam): The antecedent of a conditional proposition, the clause that comes before 'then'.
التالي (al-tālī): The consequent of a conditional proposition, the clause that follows 'then'.
القضية الكاذبة (al-qadiyya al-kādhiba): A false proposition; here specifically a conditional proposition that fails the required necessitation between antecedent and consequent.
اللزوم الناقص (al-luzūm al-nāqiṣ): Incomplete necessitation: when the consequent follows from the antecedent but the antecedent does not follow from the consequent, typically because the consequent is more general. [Note: This term is standard in later logic works for non-reversible implication.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) When both parts are true
D) When both parts are false

Key Takeaway
A conditional is true only if the consequent necessarily follows; incomplete necessitation occurs when the consequent is more general and the implication is not reversible.

Genuine Disjunctive Propositions: Conditions of Conjunction and Void
Author defines disjunctive and divides into genuine and non-genuine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a genuine disjunctive proposition by checking that it prevents both conjunction and void between its parts.

Core Idea
Genuine disjunctives block both conjunction and void — no two parts true together, no all false together.

This passage defines the disjunctive proposition and divides it into genuine and non-genuine. The author focuses on the genuine type, which uses the particle 'imma…imma' (either…or) and requires two strict conditions: the parts cannot all be true together (prevention of conjunction) and cannot all be false together (prevention of void). Understanding these conditions is essential for analyzing exclusive and exhaustive categories in Islamic logic and sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The passage defines the disjunctive proposition (al-qadiyya al-munfaṣila). Find the Arabic term and note that it expresses opposition between propositions.
2
Extract the defining conditions of genuine disjunctives
The author says a genuine disjunctive uses 'imma…imma' and prevents both jam' (conjunction) and khulu (void). Restate these two conditions in your own words.
3
Examine the two-part subcase
When the proposition has two parts, two possibilities: either the parts are direct contradictories (e.g., even/not even) or they are mutually exclusive and exhaustive like even/odd. Note that in the second case, each part is logically equivalent to the contradictory of the other.
4
Examine the multi-part subcase
With more than two parts, the parts can be finite or infinite. Example: 'The moving thing is either a mineral, a plant, or an animal' (finite); 'The number is 2, 3, 4, …' (infinite). Both satisfy the conditions.
5
Distinguish from non-genuine
Non-genuine disjunctives only satisfy one condition — for example, they may prevent conjunction but not void, or vice versa. Compare with the genuine examples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القضية المنفصلة فهي ما توجب أو تسلب عناد قضية لأخرى، لا كما في قولنا: (الزوج معاند للفرد ومنفصل عنه أو ليس معانداً فإن النسبة بينهما وإن كانت بالعناد فليست القضية إلا حَمْلية، وهي وإن فارقت المتصلة في أن النسبة الإيجابية لها بالانفصال، وللمتصلة بالاتصال، فمشاركة لها في النسبة الخبرية لكل واحد من جزأيها عند التحليل، وإنها منقسمة على حسب انقسام المتصلة إلى حقيقي وغير حقيقي، فأما الحقيقي من المنفصلة فما لفظه (إما) وإما فيها دالة على المنع من الجمع بين الأجزاء، والخلو منها، وهي إما أن تكون ذات جزأين فقط أو أكثر، فإن كان الأول فإما أن يكون أحد الجزأين نقيضاً للآخر كقولنا: العدد إما أن يكون زوجاً أو لا يكون زوجاً وإما أن يكون أحد الجزأين لازماً لنقيض الجزء الآخر وهو مساوٍ له في العموم والخصوص كقولنا : العدد إما زوج وإما فرد فإنه وإن امتنع الجمع بين الزوج والفرد والخلو منهما، فالفرد لازم لنقيض الزوج وهو لا زوج ومساوٍ له، وكذلك الزوج بالنسبة إلى الفرد. وإن كان الثاني وهو أن تكون القضية أكثر من جزأين، فالأجزاء إما متناهية أو غير متناهية فالمتناهية كقولنا: (المتحرك إما جماد أو نبات أو حيوان وغير المتناهية كقولنا : العدد إما اثنان أو ثلاثة أو أربعة إلى غير النهاية).
As for the disjunctive proposition, it is what affirms or negates the opposition of one proposition to another, not as in our saying: 'The even is opposite to the odd and separate from it' or 'it is not opposite,' for the relation between them, even if it is by opposition, the proposition is only categorical (ḥamliyya). And although it differs from the conditional in that the positive relation for it is by separation, and for the conditional by connection, it shares with it in the predicative relation for each of its parts upon analysis, and it is divided according to the division of the conditional into genuine and non-genuine. As for the genuine of the disjunctive, it is that whose wording is 'either...or' (imma...imma) indicating prevention of conjunction between the parts and void from them. And it is either having only two parts or more. If the first, then either one of the two parts is the contradictory of the other, as in our saying: 'The number is either even or not even,' or one of the two parts is necessary for the contradictory of the other part, and it is equal to it in generality and specificity, as in our saying: 'The number is either even or odd,' for although conjunction between even and odd and void from both is prevented, the odd is necessary for the contradictory of the even (i.e., not even) and is equal to it, and similarly the even with respect to the odd. If the second (i.e., the proposition has more than two parts), then the parts are either finite or infinite. Finite, as in our saying: 'The moving thing is either a mineral, a plant, or an animal.' Infinite, as in our saying: 'The number is either two, three, four, and so on without end.'
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage defines the disjunctive proposition (al-qadiyya al-munfaṣila) and distinguishes it from a categorical proposition that merely expresses opposition. The author notes that while a disjunctive proposition differs from a conditional in that its connective relation is separation (infiṣāl) rather than connection (ittiṣāl), it shares the same basic structure of two parts. He then divides disjunctives into genuine (ḥaqīqī) and non-genuine (ghayr ḥaqīqī). A genuine disjunctive is one that uses the particle 'imma...imma' (either...or) to indicate both prevention of conjunction (mani' al-jam') and prevention of void (mani' al-khulu) between the parts. He then details the genuine type with two parts: either the parts are direct contradictories (e.g., 'number is either even or not even') or they are such that each part is logically equivalent to the contradictory of the other, meaning they are mutually exclusive and exhaustive (e.g., 'number is either even or odd'). In the second case, odd is the same as 'not even', and vice versa, so they are equal in generality. He then discusses genuine disjunctives with more than two parts, which can be finite (e.g., moving thing is mineral, plant, or animal) or infinite (e.g., number is 2, 3, 4, ...). The key takeaway is that genuine disjunctives require both exclusivity (no conjunction) and exhaustiveness (no void).

Key Terms
القضية المنفصلة
الحقيقي
منع الجمع
منع الخلو
النقيض

D) 'The number is either two, three, or four'

Key Takeaway
A genuine disjunctive proposition requires both prevention of conjunction and prevention of void, signaled by the particle 'imma…imma'.

Non-Genuine Disjunctive: Mani'at al-Jam' (Preventing Conjunction Only)
Author defines non-genuine disjunctive that prevents conjunction only, with examples of two or more parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a non-genuine disjunctive (māni'at al-jam') and verify that in a two-part case one part is more specific than the contradictory of the other.

Core Idea
Mani'at al-jam' prevents parts from being true together, but allows all parts to be false.

The passage distinguishes between genuine and non-genuine disjunctives. Here the author focuses on the type that prevents conjunction only, not void. This logical form appears frequently in Islamic legal and theological arguments where one wants to reject that two attributes can coexist, without requiring that at least one must hold.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive as non-genuine
Check whether the disjunctive explicitly or contextually indicates that the parts cannot be true together (jam') but can all be false (khuluw). If only conjunction is prevented, it is māni'at al-jam'.
2
Check the number of parts
Determine if the disjunctive has two parts or more than two parts. The author provides specific conditions for each case.
3
For two-part disjunctive: analyse the relation between parts
One part must be more specific (akhaṣṣ) than the contradictory of the other part. Neither part should be a contradictory, a necessary equivalent, or more general than the other's contradictory. This ensures that if one part is false, the other is not necessarily true.
Example: 'mineral' is more specific than 'not animal', and 'animal' is more specific than 'not mineral'.
4
For multi-part disjunctive: verify the same logical effect
When there are more than two parts, the disjunctive still prevents conjunction of all parts simultaneously, but allows that none of the listed parts holds. The author gives the example of colors.
Example: 'black, white, or yellow' – the object could be another color like red, so all parts false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الناقص غير الحقيقي من المنفصلة فما كانت لفظة (إما) وإما فيه دالة على المنع من الجمع دون الخلو، أو الخلو دون الجمع، فما كانت مانعة الجمع دون الخلو فإما ذات جزأين أو أكثر، فإن كانت ذات جزأين فلا بد وأن يكون أحد الجزأين أخص من نقيض الجزء الآخر، ولا يكون نقيضاً للجزء الآخر ولا لازماً مساوياً ولا أعمّ، وذلك كقولنا: (هذا المتحرك إما جماد وإما حيوان قصداً للرد على من أوجب الاجتماع بينهما، فإنه وإن امتنع الجمع بينهما فليس يمتنع الخلو منهما، بأن يكون المتحرك المشار إليه ليس بحيوان ولا جماد بل نبات، وكل واحد من الجزأين أخص من نقيض الآخر، فإن نقيض الحيوان لا حيوان أعم من الجماد ونقيض الجماد لا جماد وهو أعم من الحيوان، وإن كانت أكثر من جزأين كقولنا: هذا الشيء إما أسود وإما أبيض وإما أصفر قصداً للرد على من أوجب الاجتماع بينهما.
As for the incomplete non-genuine of the disjunctive, it is that whose wording (imma...imma) indicates prevention of conjunction without prevention of void, or prevention of void without prevention of conjunction. So what is a preventer of conjunction without void is either having two parts or more. If it has two parts, then one of the two parts must be more specific than the contradictory of the other part, and it is not a contradictory of the other part, nor a necessary equivalent, nor more general. That is as in our saying: 'This moving thing is either a mineral or an animal,' intended to refute someone who asserted conjunction between them. For although conjunction between them is prevented, void from them is not prevented, because the designated moving thing could be neither animal nor mineral but a plant. And each of the two parts is more specific than the contradictory of the other, for the contradictory of 'animal' (i.e., not animal) is more general than 'mineral', and the contradictory of 'mineral' (i.e., not mineral) is more general than 'animal'. And if it has more than two parts, as in our saying: 'This thing is either black or white or yellow,' intended to refute someone who asserted conjunction between them.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage describes the first type of non-genuine disjunctive proposition: the one that prevents conjunction (jam') but does not prevent void (khulu). That is, the parts cannot all be true together, but they can all be false together. The author explains that when such a disjunctive has two parts, one part must be more specific (akhaṣṣ) than the contradictory of the other part. This ensures that the two parts are not exhaustive: if one is false, the other is not necessarily true. For example, in 'This moving thing is either a mineral or an animal,' it is possible that the moving thing is neither a mineral nor an animal (e.g., it could be a plant), so both parts can be false. The author notes that each part is more specific than the contradictory of the other: 'mineral' is more specific than 'not animal', and 'animal' is more specific than 'not mineral'. For more than two parts, the example is 'This thing is either black, white, or yellow' — here too, the parts cannot all be true simultaneously, but they can all be false if the thing is some other color like red. The purpose of such statements is to refute someone who wrongly thinks that two or more of these properties can coexist in the same subject.

Key Terms
غير الحقيقي
مانعة الجمع دون الخلو
أخص

C) The parts can be both true and both false

Key Takeaway
A mani'at al-jam' disjunctive prohibits the conjunction of its parts but allows them all to be false, requiring in a two-part case that one part be more specific than the other's contradictory.

Understanding the Preventer of Void Only
Author discusses the preventer of void only with condition for two parts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a disjunctive that prevents void only and verify the condition that one part is a general necessary for the contradictory of the other.

Core Idea
A disjunctive that prevents void only ensures at least one part is true, but may allow both to be true.

The second type of non-genuine disjunctive is one that prevents the emptiness of truth (khulu) but does not prevent the conjunction of parts (jam'). This is a common logical form in Islamic legal reasoning where a ruling is presented as one of two options, with the possibility that both apply. In this lesson, you will learn to recognize this type and test its defining condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive type
Examine the disjunctive proposition. If it states that at least one part must be true but does not forbid both from being true, then it prevents void only. In contrast, a genuine disjunctive (mani'at al-jam' wa al-khulu) would prevent both void and conjunction.
The Arabic phrase 'مانعة الخلو دون الجمع' explicitly indicates this type.
2
Understand the condition for two parts
The author stipulates that for a two-part disjunctive of this type, one part must be a general necessary (lāzim 'āmm) for the contradictory of the other. This means that if the contradictory of one part is true, the other part is necessarily true. For example, in 'This thing is either black or white,' if it is not black, then it must be white (preventing void).
The 'general necessary' relation ensures that both parts cannot be false together.
3
Check for intermediate cases
The example 'black or white' becomes false if a third color (e.g., yellow) is possible, because then both 'not black' and 'not white' are true, making the disjunctive false. This shows that the disjunctive prevents void only when no alternative outside the stated parts exists.
The author mentions 'al-'adamayn' (the two non-existences) referring to the negations of both parts.
4
Distinguish from genuine disjunctive
Unlike the genuine disjunctive, this type does not assert that the parts are mutually exclusive. Both could be true simultaneously, though the example 'black or white' in common usage implies exclusivity; the logical definition here focuses on the void-preventing aspect.
This nuance is key for applications in Islamic jurisprudence where multiple legal rulings may apply.

The Complete True Disjunctive (al-Munfasila al-Tamma al-Haqiqiyya)
Author defines the complete disjunctive and distinguishes two-part from multi-part cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish complete true disjunctive propositions, including two-part and multi-part forms, based on the conditions of mutual exclusivity and collective exhaustiveness.

Core Idea
A complete true disjunctive prohibits both conjunction and emptiness: parts are mutually exclusive and collectively exhaustive.

In this passage, the author defines the complete true disjunctive (al-munfasila al-tamma al-haqiqiyya). This type of disjunctive proposition is characterized by the fact that its parts can neither be true together (jam‘) nor false together (khuluw). The author illustrates this with the classic example 'The number is either even or odd', then extends the concept to propositions with more than two parts, distinguishing finite from infinite cases. Understanding this category is essential for analyzing disjunctive syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining condition
In the two-part case, note the two impossibilities: jam‘ (both true) and khuluw (both false). This is the core of the complete true disjunctive.
2
Examine the two-part example
The example 'the number is either even or odd' shows mutual exclusivity and exhaustiveness. Verify that each part is necessary for the negation of the other and equivalent in scope.
3
Recognize multi-part cases
The author extends to propositions with more than two parts, dividing into finite and infinite. Understand that the same conditions apply: no two parts can be true together, and one part must be true.
4
Analyze the examples
Finite: 'the moving thing is either inanimate, plant, or animal'. Infinite: 'the number is either two, three, four... ad infinitum'. These satisfy the complete true disjunctive conditions.
5
Distinguish from other disjunctives
Compare with the incomplete disjunctive where only jam‘ or khuluw is prohibited, but not both. This ensures precision in logical analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإما أن يكون أحد الجزأين لازماً لنقيض الجزء الآخر وهو مساوٍ له في العموم والخصوص كقولنا : العدد إما زوج وإما فرد فإنه وإن امتنع الجمع بين الزوج والفرد والخلو منهما، فالفرد لازم لنقيض الزوج وهو لا زوج ومساوٍ له، وكذلك الزوج بالنسبة إلى الفرد. وإن كان الثاني وهو أن تكون القضية أكثر من جزأين، فالأجزاء إما متناهية أو غير متناهية فالمتناهية كقولنا: المتحرك إما جماد أو نبات أو حيوان وغير المتناهية كقولنا : العدد إما اثنان أو ثلاثة أو أربعة إلى غير النهاية.
And either one of the two parts is necessary for the negation of the other part and is equivalent to it in generality and specificity, like our saying: 'The number is either even or odd.' For although conjunction between even and odd and emptiness from both are impossible, odd is necessary for the negation of even—which is 'not even'—and equivalent to it, and likewise even with respect to odd. And if it is the second [case], that the proposition has more than two parts, then the parts are either finite or infinite. The finite is like our saying: 'The moving thing is either inanimate, or plant, or animal.' And the infinite is like our saying: 'The number is either two, or three, or four, ad infinitum.'
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage defines the complete true disjunctive (al-munfasila al-tamma al-haqiqiyya). The author begins by describing the two-part case: each part is necessary for the negation of the other and equivalent in scope. The example given is 'the number is either even or odd' (al-‘adad immā zawj wa immā fard). Here, even and odd are mutually exclusive and exhaustive; conjunction is impossible (lā yumkin al-jam‘) and emptiness is impossible (lā yumkin al-khuluw). The author notes that 'odd' is necessary for the negation of even (lā zawj) and equivalent. Then the author addresses the case of more than two parts, dividing into finite and infinite. The finite example is 'the moving thing is either inanimate, plant, or animal' (al-mutaḥarrik immā jamād aw nabāt aw ḥayawān). The infinite example is 'the number is either two, three, four... ad infinitum' (al-‘adad immā ithnān aw thalātha aw arba‘a ilā ghayr al-nihāya). These are all instances where both conjunction and emptiness are impossible, fulfilling the condition of the complete true disjunctive.

Key Terms
المنفصلة التامة الحقيقية
جمع
خلو

D) Both conjunction and emptiness are impossible.

Key Takeaway
The complete true disjunctive prohibits both conjunction (jam‘) and emptiness (khuluw): its parts are mutually exclusive and collectively exhaustive, whether two or more.

Man‘at al-Jam‘ and Man‘at al-Khuluw: Incomplete Disjunctives
Author defines incomplete disjunctives, distinguishing man‘at al-jam‘ and man‘at al-khuluw with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish between māni‘at al-jam‘ (preventing conjunction) and māni‘at al-khuluw (preventing emptiness) in incomplete disjunctive propositions.

Core Idea
Incomplete disjunctives prevent either conjunction or emptiness, but not both.

This passage defines incomplete disjunctives as those that prevent only one of two logical conditions: either conjunction (both parts true) or emptiness (both parts false). The author distinguishes between māni‘at al-jam‘ and māni‘at al-khuluw, providing examples such as 'This moving thing is either inanimate or animal.' Understanding this distinction is essential for analyzing the truth conditions of disjunctives in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Incomplete Disjunctive
Look for a disjunctive proposition using 'immā... immā' and determine if it prevents both conjunction and emptiness or only one. If only one, it is incomplete.
2
Determine the Type
If the disjunctive prevents conjunction (both parts cannot be true simultaneously) but allows emptiness (both can be false), it is māni‘at al-jam‘. If it prevents emptiness (both cannot be false) but allows conjunction (both can be true), it is māni‘at al-khuluw.
Use the author's examples to verify.
3
Apply the Author's Criteria
For two-part māni‘at al-jam‘, check that each part is more specific than the negation of the other. For two-part māni‘at al-khuluw, check that one part is a general necessary for the negation of the other.
Example: 'This moving thing is either inanimate or animal' – 'inanimate' is more specific than 'not animal'.
4
Practice with Multiple Parts
Extend the analysis to disjunctives with more than two parts, like 'This thing is either black, white, or yellow.' Recognize the same principles apply.

وأما الناقص غير الحقيقي من المنفصلة فما كانت لفظة (إما) وإما فيه دالة على المنع من الجمع دون الخلو، أو الخلو دون الجمع، فما كانت مانعة الجمع دون الخلو فإما ذات جزأين أو أكثر، فإن كانت ذات جزأين فلا بد كقولنا: (هذا الشيء إما لا أسود وإما لا أبيض وإما لا أصفر) فإنه وإن امتنع الخلو منها فقد لا يمتنع الجمع بينهما، بأن يكون أحمر أو أخضر، فالقسم الأول وهو التام يفارق القسمين الآخرين في أنه يمتنع فيه الجمع والخلو، ويفارق الثاني في المنع من الخلو ويشاركه في المنع من الجمع، ويفارق الثالث في المنع من الجمع، ويشاركه في المنع من الخلو ويفارق الثاني للثالث في امتناع الجمع ، ويشاركه في امتناع استعمال لا اختصاراً وتعويلاً على فهم الذهن ،له وإقامة اللازم مقامه، وذلك في مانع من الجمع دون الخلو أن يقال هذا الشيء إما أن يكون نباتاً وإما أن لا يكون نباتاً) وإن كان لا يكون نباتاً فقد يصح أن يكون حيواناً، فالقول الأول منفصل تام حقيقي، والثاني متصل لكنه لما كان قد يصح أن يكون حيواناً لازماً من لا نبات حذف لا نبات وأقيم مقامه. ما هو وأما في ما هو مانع من الخلو دون الجمع فإن يقال هذا الشيء إما أن لا يكون وإما (حيوان أنه إما جماد أو ليس بجماد وإن كان ليس بجماد فإما حيوان وإما غير (حيوان فليس ذلك مما يتوقف الذهن في النسبة عليه، بخلاف هذين القسمين.
As for the incomplete, non-true disjunctive, it is that in which the expression 'immā... immā' indicates the prevention of conjunction without emptiness, or emptiness without conjunction. That which prevents conjunction without emptiness is either of two parts or more. If it is of two parts, then one of the two parts must be more specific than the negation of the other part, and it is neither the negation of the other part, nor a necessary equivalent, nor more general. An example is our saying: 'This moving thing is either inanimate or animal,' intending to refute someone who posits conjunction between them. For although conjunction between them is impossible, emptiness from them is not impossible, in that the indicated moving thing is neither animal nor inanimate but rather a plant. And each of the two parts is more specific than the negation of the other, for the negation of animal—'not animal'—is more general than inanimate, and the negation of inanimate—'not inanimate'—is more general than animal. And if it has more than two parts, like our saying: 'This thing is either black or white or yellow,' intending to refute someone who posits conjunction between them. And if it prevents emptiness without conjunction, then the proposition is either of two parts or more. If it is of exactly two parts, then one of the two parts must be a general necessary for the negation of the other part, like our saying: 'This thing is either black or white,' meaning that it is not empty of one of the two negations. For if it is white, then it is not black, and if it is black, then it is not white. And if there is an intermediary, such as being yellow or red, then the two negations have come together, so it is not empty of one of the two negations while conjunction between them is conceivable. And the negation of our saying 'not black and not white'—which is the other part—is more general than black, which is the negation of 'not black,' and likewise on the other side. And in this is our saying: 'Either Zaid is in the sea or he does not drown,' for the negation of 'does not drown' is a meaning more general than the sea, since 'not drowning' may occur in the sea or not in the sea. And if it has more than two parts, like our saying: 'This thing is either not black or not white or not yellow,' for although emptiness from them is impossible, conjunction between them may not be impossible, such as being red or green. So the first division—the complete—differs from the other two in that conjunction and emptiness are impossible in it. It differs from the second in the prevention of emptiness and shares with it the prevention of conjunction. It differs from the third in the prevention of conjunction and shares with it the prevention of emptiness. The second differs from the third in the impossibility of conjunction and shares with it the impossibility of not being empty? Actually, in both, the usage of 'lā yakhlū' is not like that in the complete disjunctive, for there it indicates the prevention of both conjunction and emptiness together, unlike here. And in that each of them contains an implicit (idmār) such that if it were made explicit, the proposition would revert to a disjunctive and a conditional, omitted for brevity and relying on the understanding of the mind, by substituting the consequent for the antecedent. That is, in the case of preventing conjunction without emptiness, one would say: 'This thing is either a plant or not a plant.' And if it is not a plant, it may be an animal. So the first statement is a complete true disjunctive, and the second is a conditional. But since 'animal' may be a necessary consequent of 'not plant,' 'not plant' is omitted and replaced by it. And as for what prevents emptiness without conjunction, one would say: 'This thing is either not black or black.' And if it is black, then it is not white. So the first statement is also a complete true disjunctive, and the second is a conditional. And since 'not white' is a necessary consequent of 'being black,' 'being black' is omitted and replaced by 'not white.' And such implicitness, although possible in the complete true disjunctive and its reversion to a disjunctive and a conditional, as in our saying: 'The number is either even or odd'—one could say: 'The number is either even or not even, and if it is not even, then it is odd'—and likewise if we say: 'This thing is either inanimate or plant or animal'—one could say: 'It is either inanimate or not inanimate, and if it is not inanimate, then it is either animal or non-animal'—that is not something on which the mind depends for the relationship, unlike these two divisions.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage defines the incomplete disjunctive (al-munfasila al-naqiṣa). The author first distinguishes two types: one that prevents conjunction only (māni‘at al-jam‘) and one that prevents emptiness only (māni‘at al-khuluw). For the first type (preventing conjunction only), the author explains that if there are two parts, each part must be more specific than the negation of the other. The example is 'This moving thing is either inanimate or animal,' where conjunction is impossible (something cannot be both inanimate and animal simultaneously), but emptiness is possible (it could be a plant). The relationship is that 'inanimate' is more specific than 'not animal' (since not animal includes inanimate and plant), and similarly 'animal' is more specific than 'not inanimate.' If there are more than two parts, the same logic applies, as in 'This thing is either black, white, or yellow'—conjunction is impossible (cannot be two colors at once), but emptiness is possible (it could be red). For the second type (preventing emptiness only), the author explains that with two parts, one part must be a general necessary for the negation of the other. The example is 'This thing is either black or white,' meaning it cannot be devoid of both (i.e., it must be either black or white, but could be both? Actually the author says 'not empty of one of the two negations,' meaning that it is either black or white, but if there is an intermediary like yellow, both negations ('not black' and 'not white') are true, so it is not empty of them. The key is that emptiness is prevented (the thing must have one of the two properties), but conjunction is possible? Wait, the example 'Zaid is either in the sea or he does not drown' prevents emptiness (he must be either in the sea or not drowning, but could be both if he is not in the sea and not drowning? Actually, the author says the negation of 'does not drown' is more general than 'sea,' because 'not drown' can happen in the sea or outside. This is a bit confusing. Then the author discusses the case of more than two parts with negative examples like 'This thing is either not black, not white, or not yellow'—emptiness is impossible (it must be devoid of at least one color? Actually it means it must lack at least one of these colors), but conjunction may be possible (it could be red, lacking all three?). Then the author compares the three types (complete, man‘at al-jam‘, man‘at al-khuluw) and highlights their differences. Finally, the author explains the concept of idmār (implicitness) in these incomplete disjunctives. Basically, an incomplete disjunctive can be analyzed as a combination of a complete disjunctive and a conditional, with one part omitted for brevity. For example, in man‘at al-jam‘: 'This thing is either a plant or not a plant' (complete disjunctive) and 'If it is not a plant, it may be an animal' (conditional). Since 'animal' is a necessary consequent of 'not plant' in this context, 'not plant' is omitted and replaced by 'animal,' giving the surface form 'This is either a plant or an animal' which actually means 'This is either a plant or not a plant, and if not plant then animal.' Similarly for man‘at al-khuluw: 'This is either not black or black' (complete disjunctive) and 'If it is black, then it is not white.' Since 'not white' is a necessary consequent of 'black,' 'black' is omitted and replaced by 'not white,' giving 'This is either not black or not white' actually meaning 'This is either not black or black, and if black then not white.' The author notes that such implicitness could also be applied to complete disjunctives, but the mind does not need it there, unlike the incomplete types.

Idmār in Incomplete Disjunctives: Reduction to Complete Disjunctive and Conditional
Author explains how incomplete disjunctives can be reduced to a complete disjunctive and a conditional via idmār.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze an incomplete disjunctive as a combination of a complete disjunctive and a conditional via the process of idmār (implicit omission).

Core Idea
Every incomplete disjunctive implicitly contains a complete disjunctive and a conditional.

The passage explains that incomplete disjunctives contain an implicit structure: they can be expressed as a complete true disjunctive followed by a conditional. For example, 'This thing is either a plant or an animal' actually means 'This is either a plant or not a plant, and if it is not a plant then it is an animal.' This technique, called idmār, relies on the understanding of the mind to supply the omitted part.

Guided Path
1
Identify an Incomplete Disjunctive
Pick an incomplete disjunctive from the passage, such as 'This thing is either a plant or an animal.'
2
Find the Implicit Complete Disjunctive
Recognize that the surface form can be rephrased as 'A or not A' where 'not A' is the broader category. For the example, 'This is either a plant or not a plant' is the complete disjunctive.
This is the first part of the idmār.
3
Add the Conditional
The omitted part is a conditional: 'If not A, then B.' Here, 'If it is not a plant, then it is an animal.' This conditional must hold in the context.
4
Verify the Substitution
Check that 'B' (animal) is a necessary consequent of 'not A' (not plant) in the given context, allowing the replacement of 'not A' with 'B' in the surface form.
The passage says the substitution is made for brevity, relying on the mind's understanding.

As for the incomplete, non-true disjunctive, it is that in which the expression 'immā... immā' indicates the prevention of conjunction without emptiness, or emptiness without conjunction. That which prevents conjunction without emptiness is either of two parts or more. If it is of two parts, then one of the two parts must be more specific than the negation of the other part, and it is neither the negation of the other part, nor a necessary equivalent, nor more general. An example is our saying: 'This moving thing is either inanimate or animal,' intending to refute someone who posits conjunction between them. For although conjunction between them is impossible, emptiness from them is not impossible, in that the indicated moving thing is neither animal nor inanimate but rather a plant. And each of the two parts is more specific than the negation of the other, for the negation of animal—'not animal'—is more general than inanimate, and the negation of inanimate—'not inanimate'—is more general than animal. And if it has more than two parts, like our saying: 'This thing is either black or white or yellow,' intending to refute someone who posits conjunction between them. And if it prevents emptiness without conjunction, then the proposition is either of two parts or more. If it is of exactly two parts, then one of the two parts must be a general necessary for the negation of the other part, like our saying: 'This thing is either black or white,' meaning that it is not empty of one of the two negations. For if it is white, then it is not black, and if it is black, then it is not white. And if there is an intermediary, such as being yellow or red, then the two negations have come together, so it is not empty of one of the two negations while conjunction between them is conceivable. And the negation of our saying 'not black and not white'—which is the other part—is more general than black, which is the negation of 'not black,' and likewise on the other side. And in this is our saying: 'Either Zaid is in the sea or he does not drown,' for the negation of 'does not drown' is a meaning more general than the sea, since 'not drowning' may occur in the sea or not in the sea. And if it has more than two parts, like our saying: 'This thing is either not black or not white or not yellow,' for although emptiness from them is impossible, conjunction between them may not be impossible, such as being red or green. So the first division—the complete—differs from the other two in that conjunction and emptiness are impossible in it. It differs from the second in the prevention of emptiness and shares with it the prevention of conjunction. It differs from the third in the prevention of conjunction and shares with it the prevention of emptiness. The second differs from the third in the impossibility of conjunction and shares with it the impossibility of not being empty? Actually, in both, the usage of 'lā yakhlū' is not like that in the complete disjunctive, for there it indicates the prevention of both conjunction and emptiness together, unlike here. And in that each of them contains an implicit (idmār) such that if it were made explicit, the proposition would revert to a disjunctive and a conditional, omitted for brevity and relying on the understanding of the mind, by substituting the consequent for the antecedent. That is, in the case of preventing conjunction without emptiness, one would say: 'This thing is either a plant or not a plant.' And if it is not a plant, it may be an animal. So the first statement is a complete true disjunctive, and the second is a conditional. But since 'animal' may be a necessary consequent of 'not plant,' 'not plant' is omitted and replaced by it. And as for what prevents emptiness without conjunction, one would say: 'This thing is either not black or black.' And if it is black, then it is not white. So the first statement is also a complete true disjunctive, and the second is a conditional. And since 'not white' is a necessary consequent of 'being black,' 'being black' is omitted and replaced by 'not white.' And such implicitness, although possible in the complete true disjunctive and its reversion to a disjunctive and a conditional, as in our saying: 'The number is either even or odd'—one could say: 'The number is either even or not even, and if it is not even, then it is odd'—and likewise if we say: 'This thing is either inanimate or plant or animal'—one could say: 'It is either inanimate or not inanimate, and if it is not inanimate, then it is either animal or non-animal'—that is not something on which the mind depends for the relationship, unlike these two divisions.

Commentary
This passage defines the incomplete disjunctive (al-munfasila al-naqiṣa). The author first distinguishes two types: one that prevents conjunction only (māni‘at al-jam‘) and one that prevents emptiness only (māni‘at al-khuluw). For the first type (preventing conjunction only), the author explains that if there are two parts, each part must be more specific than the negation of the other. The relationship is that 'inanimate' is more specific than 'not animal' (since not animal includes inanimate and plant), and similarly 'animal' is more specific than 'not inanimate.' For the second type (preventing emptiness only), the author explains that with two parts, one part must be a general necessary for the negation of the other. The example is 'This thing is either black or white,' meaning it cannot be devoid of both (i.e., it must be either black or white, but could be both? Actually the author says 'not empty of one of the two negations,' meaning that it is either black or white, but if there is an intermediary like yellow, both negations ('not black' and 'not white') are true, so it is not empty of them. Actually it means it must lack at least one of these colors), but conjunction may be possible (it could be red, lacking all three?). Then the author compares the three types (complete, man‘at al-jam‘, man‘at al-khuluw) and highlights their differences. Basically, an incomplete disjunctive can be analyzed as a combination of a complete disjunctive and a conditional, with one part omitted for brevity. For example, in man‘at al-jam‘: 'This thing is either a plant or not a plant' (complete disjunctive) and 'If it is not a plant, it may be an animal' (conditional). Classification of Disjunctive Propositions: Complete and Incomplete
Author classifies disjunctive propositions into three types based on prevention of combination and void.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the three types of disjunctive propositions and identify which type a given disjunctive statement belongs to based on whether it prevents combination, void, or both.

Core Idea
Disjunctive propositions differ: some prevent both options being true, some prevent both being false, some do both.

In the previous lessons, we learned about conditional propositions. Now we turn to disjunctive propositions (al-munfasilat), which connect two or more parts using 'إما' (imma). The author of the Daqa'iq al-Haqa'iq classifies them into three types based on two properties: prevention of combination (mani' al-jam') and prevention of void (mani' al-khuluw). Understanding these types is essential for correctly analysing disjunctive arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Define the two properties
Understand what 'prevention of combination' (mani' al-jam') and 'prevention of void' (mani' al-khuluw) mean. Combination is impossible if the parts cannot all be true at the same time; void is impossible if the parts cannot all be false at the same time.
These are the criteria for classification.
2
Identify the three types
Based on these two properties, the author defines three types: (1) Complete (tamm): both combination and void are impossible. (2) Mani' al-jam' duna al-khuluw: only combination is prevented; void is not prevented. (3) Mani' al-khuluw duna al-jam': only void is prevented; combination is not prevented.
Memorise the characteristics of each type.
3
Analyse the examples in the passage
For the complete type, the author gives 'The number is either even or odd' — it cannot be both, and must be one. For the type preventing void only, he gives 'This thing is either not black, not white, or not yellow' — here void is impossible (at least one is true), but combination is possible (all can be true if the object is red). For the type preventing combination only, the example 'This thing is either a plant or not a plant' contains an implicit conditional.
Notice how the complete disjunctive prevents both.
4
Recognise the implicit conditional
The author shows that incomplete disjunctives contain an implicit conditional. For a disjunctive that prevents combination only, e.g., 'This thing is either a plant or not a plant', the second part implies a conditional: 'If it is not a plant, then it is an animal' (or similar). This implicit conditional can be made explicit.
This reduction helps in logical analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الثاني: في القضايا الشرطية
الآخر أعم من أسود الذي هو نقيض لا أسود، وكذلك في الطرف الآخر، وفي هذا قولنا: إما أن يكون زيد في البحر وإما أن لا يغرق فإن نقيض لا غرق معناه وهو أعم من البحر إذ لا غرق قد يكون في البحر، وقد يكون لا في البحر. وإن كانت أكثر من جزأين كقولنا: (هذا الشيء إما لا أسود وإما لا أبيض وإما لا أصفر) فإنه وإن امتنع الخلو منها فقد لا يمتنع الجمع بينهما، بأن يكون أحمر أو أخضر، فالقسم الأول وهو التام يفارق القسمين الآخرين في أنه يمتنع فيه الجمع والخلو، ويفارق الثاني في المنع من الخلو ويشاركه في المنع من الجمع، ويفارق الثالث في المنع من الجمع، ويشاركه في المنع من الخلو ويفارق الثاني للثالث في امتناع الجمع ، ويشاركه في امتناع استعمال لا يخلو فيهما على النحو المستعمل في المنفصلة التامة، إذ هي فيها دالة على المنع من الجمع والخلو معاً، ولا كذلك فيها وفي أن كل واحد منهما مشتمل على إضمار لو صرح به عادت القضية إلى منفصلة ومتصلة حذف اختصاراً وتعويلاً على فهم الذهن ،له وإقامة اللازم مقامه، وذلك في مانع من الجمع دون الخلو أن يقال هذا الشيء إما أن يكون نباتاً وإما أن لا يكون نباتاً) وإن كان لا يكون نباتاً فقد يصح أن يكون حيواناً، فالقول الأول منفصل تام حقيقي، والثاني متصل لكنه لما كان قد يصح أن يكون حيواناً لازماً من لا نبات حذف لا نبات وأقيم مقامه.
ما هو
وأما في ما هو مانع من الخلو دون الجمع فإن يقال هذا الشيء إما أن لا يكون أسود أو يكون أسود، وإن كان أسود فلا يكون أبيض فالقول الأول أيضاً منفصل تام ،حقيقي، والثاني متصل، ولما كان لا بياض لازماً من كون السواد حذف كون السواد وأقيم لا بياض مقامه، وما مثل هذا الإضمار وإن أمكن في المنفصل الحقيقي التام وعوده إلى منفصلة ومتصلة كما في قولنا : العدد إما زوج وإما فرد) بأن يقال إن العدد إما زوج أو ليس بزوج، وإن كان ليس بزوج فهو فرد) وكذا أن نا يقال هذا الشيء إما جماد وإما نبات وإما (حيوان أنه إما جماد أو ليس بجماد وإن كان ليس بجماد فإما حيوان وإما غير (حيوان فليس ذلك مما يتوقف الذهن في النسبة عليه، بخلاف هذين القسمين.
The second art: On conditional propositions. The other is more general than black, which is the opposite of not black, and likewise in the other end. And in this is our saying: Either Zayd is in the sea or he does not drown, for the opposite of 'does not drown' is meaning, and it is more general than the sea, since 'does not drown' may be in the sea and may not be in the sea. And if it has more than two parts, like our saying: (This thing is either not black, or not white, or not yellow) then although void from them is impossible, combination between them may not be impossible, such as it being red or green. So the first section, which is the complete, differs from the other two sections in that combination and void are impossible in it; it differs from the second in prevention of void and shares with it in prevention of combination; it differs from the third in prevention of combination and shares with it in prevention of void; and the second differs from the third in impossibility of combination and shares with it in impossibility of using 'not void' in both in the manner used in the complete disjunctive, since the complete disjunctive indicates prevention of both combination and void together, but not so in them. And in that each one of them contains an implication (idmar) such that if it were made explicit, the proposition would revert to a disjunctive and a conjunctive, omitted for brevity and relying on the understanding of the mind, and substituting the necessary for it. That is, in what prevents combination without void, one says: This thing is either a plant or not a plant, and if it is not a plant, it may be an animal. The first statement is a true complete disjunctive, and the second is a conditional. But since 'animal' may be a necessary consequent of 'not plant', 'not plant' was deleted and replaced with it. As for what prevents void without combination, one says: This thing is either not black or black, and if it is black, it is not white. The first statement is also a true complete disjunctive, and the second is a conditional. Since 'not white' is a necessary consequent of 'blackness', 'blackness' was deleted and replaced with 'not white'. And such implication, even if possible in the true complete disjunctive, reverting it to a disjunctive and a conditional, as in our saying: The number is either even or odd, by saying: The number is either even or not even, and if it is not even, it is odd. And also if we say: This thing is either inanimate or plant or animal, that is, it is either inanimate or not inanimate, and if it is not inanimate, then it is either animal or not animal. But that does not require the mind to pause regarding the relationship, unlike these two sections.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies disjunctive propositions (al-munfasilat). The author identifies three types: (1) complete disjunctive (al-tamm) where both combination (al-jam') of the parts and void (al-khuluww) from all parts are impossible, (2) the type that prevents combination only (mani' al-jam' duna al-khuluw), and (3) the type that prevents void only (mani' al-khuluw duna al-jam'). The author first illustrates with examples: 'Either Zayd is in the sea or he does not drown' shows how the contradictory of 'does not drown' is more general than 'sea'. For more than two parts, e.g., 'This thing is either not black, not white, or not yellow', the parts cannot all be false (void is prevented) but they can all be true (combination not prevented) if the object is red. The complete disjunctive differs from the other two in that it prevents both combination and void. The second type (preventing combination only) shares with the complete the prevention of combination, but differs in that it does not prevent void. The third (preventing void only) shares prevention of void but not combination. The author then explains a key reduction: the incomplete types implicitly contain a conditional proposition. For the second type (mani' al-jam' duna al-khuluw), e.g., 'This thing is either a plant or not a plant', the second part 'not a plant' implies that if it is not a plant, it could be an animal. This can be made explicit as: 'If it is not a plant, then it is an animal.' But since 'animal' is necessary from 'not plant', the conditional is omitted and the consequent 'animal' is replaced by 'not plant' in the statement. For the third type (mani' al-khuluw duna al-jam'), e.g., 'This thing is either not black or black', the second part 'black' implies that if it is black, it is not white. Again, 'not white' is necessary from 'black', so the conditional is omitted. The author notes that even for the complete disjunctive, such an implication is possible, e.g., 'The number is either even or odd' can be expanded to 'The number is either even or not even, and if not even, it is odd.' However, this expansion does not cause confusion because the relationship is clear. The distinction is important for understanding the precise logical form of disjunctive statements.

Checkpoint
A disjunctive proposition says: 'This act is either wajib or haram.' Which type of disjunctive is it if both combination and void are impossible?
A) Complete (tamm)
B) Mani' al-jam' duna al-khuluw
C) Mani' al-khuluw duna al-jam'
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
Disjunctive propositions vary in whether they prevent the parts from being true together, from being false together, or both; identifying the type is crucial for logical analysis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Take the disjunctive 'Either Zayd is in the house or he is outside.' Expand it to include the implied conditional as the author does in the passage. What is the implied conditional?
